    We have \underline{certain} ideas about what topics are suitable.
\end{example}
\textit{Numerous}, like \textit{many}, indicates a large number in an imprecise way.
\begin{example}
    I have received \underline{numerous} requests for information.
\end{example}
\textit{Various} is used to emphasize that you are referring to several different things or people.
\begin{example}
    We looked at schools in \underline{various} European countries.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{1.246} There are 3 comparatives that are determiners.

\textit{More} is used in front of plural and uncountable nouns, usually with \textit{than}, to refer to a quantity or amount of something that is greater than another quantity or amount.
\begin{example}
    He does \underline{more} hours than I do.
    
    His visit might do \underline{more} harm than good.
\end{example}
But \textit{more} is also often used to refer to an additional quantity of something rather than in comparisons.
\begin{example}
    \underline{More} teachers need to be recruited.
    
    We need \underline{more} information.
\end{example}
\textit{Less} is used to refer to an amount of something that is smaller than another amount.

\textit{Fewer} is used to refer to a group of things that is smaller than another group.

\textit{Less} is usually used before uncountable nouns and \textit{fewer} before plural nouns, but in informal English \textit{less} is also used before plural nouns.
\begin{example}
    The poor have \underline{less} access to education.
    
    $\ldots$ machinery which uses \underline{less} energy.
    
    As a result, he found \underline{less} time than he would have hoped for his hobbies.
    
    There are \underline{fewer} trees here.
\end{example}
For more information about \textbf{comparison}, see paragraphs \textbf{2.103--2.139}.

%
\noindent\fbox{1.247} Some other expressions also behave like indefinite determiners: \textit{a few, a little, a good many, a great many}.

These have a slightly different meaning from the single word determiners \textit{few, little}, and \textit{many}.

%
If you are mentioning a small number of things, but without any emphasis, you can use \textit{a few} with plural countable nouns.
\begin{example}
    They went to San Diego for \underline{a few} days.
    
    \underline{A few} years ago we set up a factory.
    
    I usually do \underline{a few} jobs for him in the house.
\end{example}
Similarly, if you are just mentioning a small amount of something without any emphasis, you can use \textit{a little} with uncountable nouns.
\begin{example}
    He spread \underline{a little} honey on a slice of bread.
    
    I have to spend \underline{a little} time in Oxford.
    
    Charles is having \underline{a little} trouble.
\end{example}
However, \textit{a good many} and \textit{a great many} are more emphatic forms of \textit{many}.
\begin{example}
    I haven't seen her for \underline{a good many} years.
    
    He wrote \underline{a great many} novels.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{1.248} Some indefinite determiners can be modified by \textit{very, too}, and \textit{far}, or sometimes by another indefinite determiner.

%
You can modify \textit{more} with numbers or with other indefinite determiners.
\begin{example}
    Downstairs there are \underline{4 more rooms}.
    
    There had been \underline{no more accidents}.
    
    You will never have to do \underline{any more work}.
\end{example}
You can use \underline{too many} or \textit{too much} to say that a quantity is more than is wanted or needed, and \textit{too few} or \textit{too little} if it is not enough.
\begin{example}
    There were \underline{too many} competitors.
    
    They gave \underline{too much} power to the Treasury.
    
    There's \underline{too little} literature involved.
\end{example}
You can use \textit{very} before \textit{few, little, many}, and \textit{much}.

You can also say \textit{a very little} or \textit{a very great many}.
\begin{example}
    \underline{Very many} women have made their mark on industry.
    
    \underline{Very few} cars had reversing lights.
    
    I had \underline{very little} money left.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{1.249} You use \textit{one} as a determiner when you have been talking or writing about a group of people or things and you want to say something about a particular member of the group.

\textit{One} is used instead of \textit{a} or \textit{an} and is slightly more emphatic.
\begin{example}
    We had \underline{1} case that dragged on for a couple of years.
    
    They criticize me all the time, wrote \underline{1} woman.
    
    I know \underline{1} household where that happened, actually.
\end{example} 
The use of \textit{one} as a \textbf{number} is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.214--2.215}.

%
\noindent\fbox{1.250} Many determiners are also \textbf{pronouns}; i.e., they can be used without a following noun.

For more information about these, see paragraphs \textbf{1.154--1.161}.

%
\noindent\fbox{1.251} Normally, a determiner is the 1st word in a noun phrase.

However, there is a class of words called \textbf{predeterminers} which can come in front of a determiner.

%
Here is a list of predeterminers:
\begin{example}
    all, both, double, half, many, quarter, twice
    
    quite, rather, such, what
\end{example}
The 1st group are used to talk about amounts or quantities.

\textit{All} can also be used to refer to every part of something.

When used with this meaning it is used with an uncountable noun.
\begin{example}
    \underline{All} the boys started to giggle.
    
    He will give you \underline{all} the information.
    
    \underline{All} these people knew each other.
    
    I shall miss \underline{all} my friends.
    
    I invited \underline{both} the boys.
    
    \underline{Both} these parties shared 1 basic belief.
    
    She paid \underline{double} the sum they asked for.
    
    I'm getting \underline{twice} the pay I used to get.
\end{example}
In the 2nd group, \textit{quite} and \textit{rather} can be used either to emphasize or to reduce the effect of what is being said.

In speech, the meaning is made clear by your tone of voice.

In writing, it is sometimes difficult to know which meaning is intended without reading more of the text.
\begin{example}
    It takes \underline{quite} a long time to get a divorce.
    
    It was \underline{quite} a shock.
    
    Seaford is \underline{rather} a pleasant town.
    
    It was \underline{rather} a disaster.
\end{example}
\textit{Such} and \textit{what} are used for emphasis.
\begin{example}
    He has \underline{such} a beautiful voice.
    
    \underline{What} a mess!
\end{example}

%------------------------------------------------------------------------------%

\chapter{Giving information about people \& things: adjectives, numbers, \& other modifiers}

\section{Introduction}
\noindent\fbox{2.1} In the previous chapter the use of nouns, pronouns, and determiners to name and identify people and things was explained.

This chapter explains ways of giving more information about the people and the things that have already been named or identified.

%
1 way of giving more information within a noun phrase about people or things is by the use of an \textbf{adjective}, e.g. \textit{small, political}, or \textit{blue}.

Adjectives can be used as modifiers of a noun or after linking verbs.

They are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.2--2.168}.

%
Sometimes, \textbf{nouns}, rather than adjectives, are used to modify the noun.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.169--2.174}.

%
There are other groups of words that are used before a noun phrase to give more information about people and things.

They are linked to the noun phrase by \textit{of}.

These include certain \textbf{indefinite determiners} e.g. \textit{many of} and \textit{some of} (explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.176--2.193}), and other expressions that are used for describing a part or amount of something, e.g. \textit{a piece of} and \textit{a bottle of} (explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.194--2.207}).

%
\textbf{Numbers} and \textbf{fractions} are also used to indicate the amount of people or things you are talking about.

Numbers are explained in paragraph \textbf{2.208--2.239} and fractions are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.250--2.249}.

%
The other way of giving more information within a noun phrase about people or things is by using a \textbf{qualifier}, i.e., giving extra information in the form of a phrase or a clause after the noun to expand its meaning.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.272--2.302}.

\section{Describing people \& things: adjectives}
\noindent\fbox{2.2} When you want to give more information about something than you can give by using a noun alone, you can use an \textbf{adjective} to identify it or describe it in more detail.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{new} idea.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{new} ideas.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{new creative} ideas.
    
    Ideas are \underline{important}.
    
    $\ldots$ to suggest that \underline{new} ideas are \underline{useful}.
\end{example}
\noindent\fbox{2.3} The most important things to notice about an adjective in English are
\begin{itemize}
    \item what structure it is in (e.g. before a noun or after a linking verb)
    \item what type of adjective it is (e.g. describing a quality or placing the noun in a particular class).
\end{itemize}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.4} [!] The form of an adjective does not change: you use the same form for singular and plural and for subject and object.
\begin{example}
    We were looking for a \underline{good} place to camp.
    
    The next \underline{good} place was 45 miles further on.
    
    \underline{Good} places to fish were hard to find.
    
    We found hardly any \underline{good} places.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.5} Adjectives are nearly always used in connection with a noun or pronoun to give information about the person, thing, or group that is being referred to.

When this information is not the main purpose of a statement, adjectives are placed in front of a noun, as in \textit{hot coffee}.

%
The use of adjectives in a noun phrase is explained in paragraph \textbf{2.19}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.6} Sometimes, however, the main purpose of a statement is to give the information expressed by an adjective.

When this happens, adjectives are placed after a \textbf{linking verb} e.g. \textit{be} or \textit{become}, as in \textit{I am cold} and \textit{He became ill}.

The use of adjectives after a linking verb is explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.122--3.137}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.7} There is a large group of adjectives that identify qualities that someone or somethings has.

This group includes words e.g. \textit{happy} and \textit{intelligent}.

These are called \textbf{qualitative adjectives}.

%
Qualitative adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.22--2.25}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.8} There is another large group of adjectives that identify someone or something as a member of a class.

This group includes words e.g. \textit{financial} and \textit{intellectual}.

These are called \textbf{classifying adjectives}.

%
Classifying adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.26--2.28}.

%
Some adjectives are both qualitative and classifying.

These are explained in paragraph \textbf{2.29}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.9} There is a small group of adjectives that identify the color of something.

This group includes words like \textit{blue} and \textit{green}.

They are called \textbf{color adjectives}.

%
Color adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.30--2.35}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.10} Another small group of adjectives are used to emphasize your feelings about the person or thing you are talking about.

These adjectives are called \textbf{emphasizing adjectives}, and they include adjectives e.g. \textit{complete, absolute}, and \textit{utter}.

%
Emphasizing adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.36--2.39}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.11} There is a small group of adjectives that are used in a very similar way to \textbf{determiners} (see paragraphs \textbf{1.162--1.251}) to make the reference more precise.

%
These are called \textbf{postdeterminers} because their place in a noun phrase is immediately after the determiner, if there is one, and before any other adjectives.

%
Postdeterminers are explained in paragraph \textbf{2.40}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.12} Most adjectives can be used either before the noun or after a linking verb.

However, there are some that can be used only in 1 position or the other.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.41--2.53}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.13} There are a few adjectives that can be used immediately after the noun.

They are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.58--2.62}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.14} When 2 or more adjectives are used in a structure, they usually occur in a particular order.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.54--2.57}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.15} There are a large number of English adjectives ending in \textit{-ing}, many of which are related to the \textbf{\textit{-ing} participle} of a verb.

In this grammar they are called \textbf{\textit{-ing} adjectives}.

%
There are also a large number of English adjectives ending in \textit{-ed}, many of which are related to the \textbf{\textit{-ed} participle} of a verb.

In this grammar they are called \textbf{\textit{-ed} adjectives}.

%
\textit{-ing} adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.63--2.76}.

\textit{-ed} adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.77--2.93}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.16} \textbf{Compound adjectives} are made up of 2 or more words, usually written with hyphens between them.

%
Compound adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.94--2.102}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.17} When you want to compare the amount of a quality that 2 or more people or things have, you can use \textbf{comparative} and \textbf{superlative} adjectives.

There are also some other ways of comparing things.

%
Comparatives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.103--2.111}, and superlatives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.113--2.122}.

Other ways of comparing things are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.123--2.139}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.18} You can also talk about the amount of a quality that something or someone has by using an adverb like \textit{totally} or \textit{mildly} with an adjective.

%
This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.141--2.168}.

\subsection{Adjective structures}
\noindent\fbox{2.19} Adjectives are used in 2 main structures.

1 of them involves adjectives coming before the noun phrase.

If you say \textit{Julia was carrying an old suitcase}, you main purpose is to say that Julia was carrying a suitcase.

The adjective \textit{old} gives more information about what kind of suitcase it was.
\begin{example}
    He was wearing a \underline{white} t-shirt.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{technical} term.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pretty little star-shaped} flower bed.
\end{example}
Most adjectives can be used in this way.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.20} The other main structure involves adjectives being used after \textbf{linking verbs} e.g. \textit{be} and \textit{become}.

Putting an adjective after a linking verb has the effect of focusing attention on the adjective.

If you say \textit{The suitcase she was carrying was old}, your main purpose is to describe the suitcase, so the focus on the adjective \textit{old}.
\begin{example}
    The roads are \underline{busy}.
    
    The house was \underline{quiet}.
    
    He became \underline{angry}.
    
    I feel \underline{cold}.
    
    Nobody seemed \underline{amused}.
\end{example}
The use of adjectives after linking verbs is explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.132--3.137}.

%
Most adjective can be used in this way.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.21} In the following examples, the 1st example in each pair shows an adjective being used before the noun, while the 2nd example shows it being used with a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    There was no \underline{clear} evidence.
    
    `That's very \underline{clear},' I said.
    
    It has been a \underline{pleasant} evening.
    
    It's not a big stream, but it's very \underline{pleasant}.
    
    She bought a loaf of \underline{white} bread.
    
    The walls were \underline{white}.
\end{example}

\subsection{Identifying qualities: \textit{a sad story, a pretty girl}}
\noindent\fbox{2.22} There are 2 main types of adjective, \textbf{qualitative} and \textbf{classifying}.

Adjectives that describe a quality that someone or something has, e.g. \textit{sad, pretty, small, happy, healthy, wealthy}, and \textit{wise}, are called \textbf{qualitative adjectives}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{sad} story.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pretty} girl.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{small} child.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{happy} mother with a \underline{healthy} baby.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{wealthy} bankers.
    
    I think it would be \underline{wise} to give up.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.23} Adjectives that describe qualities are \textbf{gradable}, which means that the person or thing referred to can have more or less of the quality mentioned.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.24} The usual way in which you show the amount of a quality that something or someone has is by using adverbs like \textit{very} and \textit{rather} in front of qualitative adjectives.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.140--2.156}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.25} The other way in which you can talk about the amount of a quality that something or someone has is by using a \textbf{comparative}, e.g. \textit{bigger} and \textit{more interesting}, or a \textbf{superlative}, e.g. \textit{the biggest} and \textit{the most interesting}.

Comparatives and superlatives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.103--2.122}.

%
Here is a list of qualitative adjectives:
\begin{example}
    active, angry, anxious, appropriate, attractive, bad, beautiful, big, brief, bright, broad, busy, calm, careful, cheap, clean, clear, close, cold, comfortable, common, complex, cool, curious, dangerous, dark, dear, deep, determined, different, difficult, dirty, dry, easy, effective, efficient, expensive, fair, familiar, famous, fast, fat, fine, firm, flat, frank, free, fresh, friendly, frightened, funny, good, great, happy, hard, heavy, high, hot, important, interesting, kind, large, late, light, likely, long, loose, loud, lovely, low, lucky, narrow, nervous, new, nice, obvious, odd, old, pale, patient, plain, pleasant, poor, popular, powerful, pretty, proud, quick, quiet, rare, reasonable, rich, rough, sad, safe, sensible, serious, sharp, shocked, short, sick, significant, silly, simple, slow, small, soft, special, steady, strange, strong, stupid, successful, suitable, sure, surprised, sweet, tall, terrible, thick, thin, tight, tiny, tired, typical, understanding, useful, violent, warm, weak, wet, wide, wild, worried, young
\end{example}

\subsection{Identifying type: \textit{financial help, abdominal pains}}
\noindent\fbox{2.26} The other main type of adjective consists of adjectives that you use to identify the type or \textit{class} that something belongs to.

E.g., if you say \textit{financial help}, you are using the adjective \textit{financial} to describe what type of help you are talking about (i.e., to \textit{classify} help).

Adjectives that are used in this way are called \textbf{classifying adjectives}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{financial} help.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{abdominal} pains.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{medieval} manuscript.
    
    $\ldots$ my \underline{daily} shower.
    
    $\ldots$ an \underline{equal} partnership.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{sufficient} amount of milk.
\end{example}
Note that \textbf{noun modifiers} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.169--2.174}) are used in a similar way to classifying adjectives.

E.g., \textit{financial matters} and \textit{money matters} are similar in both structure and meaning.

%
Here is a list of classifying adjectives:
\begin{example}
    absolute, active, actual, agricultural, alternative, annual, apparent, available, basic, central, chemical, civil, commercial, communist, conservative, cultural, daily, democratic, direct, domestic, double, due, east, eastern, economic, educational, electric, empty, external, female, financial, foreign, free, full, general, golden, historical, human, ideal, independent, industrial, inevitable, intellectual, internal, international, legal, local, magic, male, medical, mental, military, modern, moral, national, natural, negative, north, northern, nuclear, official, open, original, personal, physical, political, positive, possible, potential, private, professional, proper, psychological, public, raw, ready, real, religious, revolutionary, right, royal, rural, scientific, separate, sexual, single, social, solid, south, southern, standard, straight, sufficient, theoretical, traditional, urban, west, western, wooden, wrong
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.27} Adjectives e.g. \textit{British, American}, and \textit{Australian}, that indicate nationality or origin, are also classifying adjectives.

They start with a capital letter because they are related to names of countries.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{American} citizens.
\end{example}
Some classifying adjectives are formed from people's names, e.g. \textit{Victorian} and \textit{Shakespearean}.

They also start with a capital letter.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{Victorian} houses.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.28} Because they put something in a class, classifying adjectives are not \textbf{gradable} in the way that qualitative adjectives are.

E.g., if you do not have to pay for something, you cannot say that it is \textit{very free}, or \textit{rather free}.

Things are either in a particular class or not in it.

Therefore, classifying adjectives do not have comparatives and superlatives and are not normally used with adverbs like \textit{very} and \textit{rather}.

%
However, when you want to show that you feel strongly about what you are saying, you can use an \textit{intensifying} adverb e.g. \textit{absolutely} with a classifying adjective.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.147--2.148}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.29} Some adjectives can be either \textbf{qualitative} or \textbf{classifying} depending on the meaning.

E.g., in \textit{an emotional person, emotional} is a qualitative adjective meaning \textit{feeling or expressing strong emotions}; it has a comparative and superlative and it can be used with words like \textit{very} and \textit{rather}.

Thus, a person can be \textit{very emotional, rather emotional}, or \textit{more emotional} than someone else.

However, in \textit{the emotional needs of children, emotional} is a classifying adjective meaning \textit{relation to a person's emotions}, and so it cannot be used with words like \textit{very} or \textit{rather}, and it does not have a comparative and superlative.

%
Here is a list of adjectives that are often used both as qualitative adjectives and as classifying adjectives:
\begin{example}
    academic, conscious, dry, educational, effective, emotional, extreme, late, modern, moral, objective, ordinary, regular, religious, revolutionary, rural, scientific, secret, similar, social
\end{example}

\subsection{Identifying colors: color adjectives}
\noindent\fbox{2.30} When you want to say what color something is, you use a \textbf{color adjective}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ her \underline{blue} eyes.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{red} ribbon.
\end{example}
Here is a list of the main color adjectives:
\begin{example}
    black, blue, brown, cream, green, grey, orange, pink, purple, red, scarlet, violet, white, yellow
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.31} If you want to specify color more precisely, you use a word e.g. \textit{light, pale, dark, deep}, or \textit{bright}, in front of a color adjective.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{light brown} hair.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pale green} suit.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{dark blue} dress.
    
    $\ldots$ deep red dye.
\end{example}
These combinations sometimes have hyphens.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{light-blue} suit.
    
    $\ldots$ the plant's tiny \underline{pale-pink} flowers.
\end{example}
Note that these words cannot be used with the colors \textit{black} or \textit{white}, because you cannot have different shades of black and white.

%
If you want to talk about a color that does not have a definite name you can:
\begin{itemize}
    \item use a color adjective with \textit{-ish} added to the end
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ \underline{greenish} glass.
        
        $\ldots$ \underline{yellowish} hair.
    \end{example}
    \item combine 2 color adjectives, often with \textit{-ish} or \textit{-y} on the end of the 1st one.
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ \underline{greenish-white} flowers.
    \end{example}
\end{itemize}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.33} [$\star$] You can mix colors in these ways to produce whatever new color you are trying to describe.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.34} Color adjectives e.g. \textit{blue} and \textit{green} occasionally have comparatives and superlatives ending in \textit{-er} and \textit{-est}.
\begin{example}
    His face was \underline{redder} than usual.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{bluest} sky I have ever seen.
\end{example}
\textbf{Comparatives} and \textbf{superlatives} are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.103--2.122}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.35} Colors can also be nouns, and the main colors can also be plural nouns.
\begin{example}
    The snow shadows had turned to a deep \underline{blue}.
    
    They blended in so well with the khaki and \underline{reds} of the landscape.
    
    $\ldots$ brilliantly colored in \underline{reds}, \underline{yellows}, \underline{blacks}, and \underline{purples}.
\end{example}

\subsection{Showing strong feelings: \textit{complete, absolute}, etc.}
\noindent\fbox{2.36} You can emphasize your feelings about something that you mention by putting an adjective e.g. \textit{complete, absolute}, or \textit{utter} in front of a noun.
\begin{example}
    He made me feel like a \underline{complete} idiot.
    
    Some of it was \underline{absolute} rubbish.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{utter} despair.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{pure} bliss.
\end{example}
You generally use an adjective of this kind only when the noun shows your opinion about something.

%
Because they are used to show strong feelings, these adjectives are called \textbf{emphasizing adjectives}.

%
Here is a list of emphasizing adjectives:
\begin{example}
    absolute, complete, entire, outright, perfect, positive, pure, real, total, true, utter
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.37} A small group of adjectives ending in \textit{-ing} are used in very informal spoken English for emphasis, usually to show disapproval or contempt.
\begin{example}
    Everybody in the whole \underline{stinking} town was loaded with money.
    
    Shut that \underline{blinking} door!
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives used informally for emphasis:
\begin{example}
    blinking, blithering, blooming, blundering, crashing, flaming, freezing, piddling, raving, scalding, stinking, thumping, thundering, whopping
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.38} [!] Many of these adjectives are usually used with 1 particular noun or adjective after them: \textit{blithering idiot, blundering idiot, crashing bore, raving lunatic, thundering nuisance, freezing cold, scalding hot, piddling little$\ldots$, thumping great$\ldots$, whopping great$\ldots$}.
\begin{example}
    He's driving that car like a \underline{raving} lunatic!
    
    I've got a \underline{stinking} cold.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{piddling little} car.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.39} The word \textit{very} is sometimes used to emphasize a noun, in expressions like \textit{the very top} and \textit{the very end}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ at the \underline{very} end of the shop.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{very} bottom of the hill.
    
    These molecules were formed at the \underline{very} beginning of history.
\end{example}

\subsection{Making the reference more precise: postdeterminers}
\noindent\fbox{2.40} There is a small group of adjectives that are used in a very similar way to \textbf{determiners} (see paragraphs \textbf{1.162--1.251}) to make the reference more precise.

These are called \textbf{postdeterminers}, because their place in a noun phrase is immediately after the determiner, if there is one, and before any other adjectives.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the \underline{following} brief description.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{certain} basic human qualities.
    
    $\ldots$ improvements in the \underline{last} few years.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{further} technological advance.
    
    He wore his \underline{usual} old white coat$\ldots$
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{only} sensible thing to do.
\end{example}
You often need to make it clear precisely what you are referring to.

E.g., if you say \textit{Turn left at the tall building} someone might ask which tall building you mean.

If you say \textit{Turn left at the next tall building}, there can be no doubt which one you mean.

The postdeterminer \textit{next} picks it out precisely.

%
Here is a list of adjectives that are postdeterminers:
\begin{example}
    additional, certain, chief, entire, existing, 1st, following, further, last, main, next, only, opposite, other, particular, past, present, previous, principal, remaining, same, special, specific, usual
\end{example}
Some of these adjectives are also ordinary classifying adjectives.
\begin{example}
    He had children from a \underline{previous} marriage.
    
    There are 2 \underline{main} reasons for this.
\end{example}
Here is a list of postdeterminers that are also classifying adjectives:
\begin{example}
    additional, chief, existing, further, main, other, particular, past, previous, principal, remaining, specific
\end{example}
Adjective that are used to show the position of something are also used for precise reference.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the \underline{middle} button of her black leather coat.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{top} 100 German companies.
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives sometimes used to talk about the position of something as well as for precise reference:
\begin{example}
    left, right, upper, lower, top, bottom, middle, end, front, back
\end{example}
Postdeterminers can also be used with numbers.

This is explained in paragraph \textbf{2.219}.

\subsection{Special classes of adjectives}
\noindent\fbox{2.41} Most adjectives can be used both before the noun and after a linking verb, but there are some that are only used in 1 position or the other.

%
There are a few adjectives that are always or almost always used in front of a noun and are never or rarely used after a linking verb.

These adjectives are called \textbf{attributive adjectives}.

%
Examples are \textit{atomic} and \textit{outdoor}.

You can talk about \textit{an atomic explosion}, but you do not say \textit{The explosion was atomic}.

You can talk about \textit{outdoor pursuits}, but you do not say \textit{Their pursuits are outdoor}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.42} A few \textbf{qualitative adjectives} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.22--2.25}) are only used in front of a noun.

Here is a list of qualitative adjectives always used in this way:
\begin{example}
    adoring, belated, chequered, choked, commanding, fateful, flagrant, fleeing, knotty, paltry, punishing, ramshackle, scant, searing, thankless, unenviable
\end{example}
Most adjectives that are only used in front of a noun are \textbf{classifying adjectives} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.26--2.28}).

Here is a list of classifying adjectives used attributively:
\begin{example}
    atomic, bridal, cardiac, countless, cubic, digital, east, eastern, eventual, existing, federal, forensic, indoor, institutional, introductory, investigate, judicial, lone, maximum, nationwide, neighboring, north, northern, occasional, orchestral, outdoor, phonetic, preconceived, remedial, reproductive, smokeless, south, southern, subterranean, supplementary, underlying, west, western, woolen
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.43} There are no \textbf{color adjectives} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.30--2.35}) that are restricted to this position.

%
\textbf{Emphasizing adjectives} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.36--2.39}) are usually used in front of a noun.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.44} Some adjectives are normally used only after a linking verb and not in front of a noun.

These adjectives are called \textbf{predicative adjectives}.

%
E.g., you can say \textit{She felt glad}, but you do not normally talk about \textit{a glad woman}.

%
Here is a list of adjectives usually used in this way:
\begin{example}
    afraid, alive, alone, apart, asleep, awake, aware, content, due, glad, ill, likely, ready, safe, sorry, sure, unable, unlikely, well
\end{example}
Note that they do not have to be followed by a prepositional phrase.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.45} Some adjectives are usually followed by a prepositional phrase because their meaning would otherwise be unclear or incomplete.

E.g., you cannot simply say that someone is \textit{accustomed}.

You have to say that they are \textit{accustomed to} something.

%
The following usage note explains which prepositions you use after a particular adjective.

%
\textbf{[i] Usage note.}

%
\noindent\fbox{2.46} There are a few adjectives that are followed by the preposition \textit{to} when they are used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    She's \underline{allergic} to cats.
    
    Older people are particularly \underline{susceptible} to heart problems.
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives that are usually or always used after a linking verb and are followed by \textit{to}:
\begin{example}
    accustomed, adjacent, allergic, attributable, attuned, averse, close, conducive, devoted, impervious, injurious, integral, prone, proportional, proportionate, reconciled, related, resigned, resistant, similar, subject, subservient, susceptible, unaccustomed
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.47} There are a few adjectives that are followed by the preposition \textit{of} when they are used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    He was \underline{aware} of the danger that faced him.
    
    They seemed \underline{capable of} winning their 1st game of the season.
    
    He was \underline{devoid of} any talent whatsoever.
    
    His mind seemed to have become \underline{incapable of} any thought.
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives that are usually or always used after a linking verb and are followed by \textit{of}:
\begin{example}
    aware, bereft, capable, characteristic, desirous, devoid, fond, full, heedless, illustrative, incapable, indicative, mindful, reminiscent, representative
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.48} There are a few adjectives that are followed by the preposition \textit{with} when they are used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    His surprise became \underline{tinged with} disbelief.
    
    The plastic has to be \underline{compatible with} the body tissues that make contact with it.
    
    This way of life is \underline{fraught with} danger.
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives that are usually or always used after a linking verb and are followed by \textit{with}:
\begin{example}
    compatible, consonant, conversant, filled, fraught, riddled, tinged
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.49} Some adjectives are followed by other prepositions when they are used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    These ideas are \underline{rooted in} self-deception.
    
    Didn't you say the raid was \underline{contingent on} the weather?
    
    Darwin concluded that people were \underline{descended from} apes.
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives that are usually or always used after a linking verb and are followed by the preposition indicated:
\begin{example}
    contingent on, descended from, inherent in, lacking in, rooted in, steeped in, swathed in, unhampered by
\end{example}
In some cases, there is a choice between 2 prepositions.
\begin{example}
    Many of their courses are \underline{connected with} industry.
    
    Such names were arbitrarily given and were not \underline{connected to} any particular event.
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives that are usually or always used after a linking verb and that are followed by the prepositions shown:
\begin{example}
    answerable for/to, burdened by/with, connected to/with, dependent on/upon, immune from/to, inclined to/towards, incumbent on/upon, insensible of/to, intent on/upon, parallel to/with, reliant on/upon, stricken by/with
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.50} \textit{Different} is most commonly followed by \textit{from}.

It is also sometimes followed by \textit{to} in British English or \textit{than} in American English.
\begin{example}[AE]
    Students today are \underline{different from} the students 10 years ago.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.51} To complete the meaning of some adjectives that are used predicatively, you need to follow with a clause beginning with a \textbf{\textit{to}-infinitive}.

E.g., you cannot just say \textit{He is unable}.

You have to add a clause beginning with \textit{to}-infinitive e.g. \textit{to do}: \textit{He is unable to do it}.

\textbf{\textit{To}-infinitive clauses} are explained in the Reference section.
\begin{example}
    They were \underline{unable to help her}.
    
    I am \underline{willing to try}.
    
    She is \underline{bound to notice there's something wrong}.
    
    I'm \underline{inclined to agree with the minister}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of adjectives that are always or nearly always followed by a \textit{to}-infinitive clause:
\begin{example}
    able, bound, destined, doomed, due, fated, fit, inclined, liable, likely, loath, prepared, unable, unwilling, willing
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.52} You can also use a clause beginning with \textit{to}-infinitive after many other adjectives to give more information about something.
\begin{example}
    I was \underline{afraid to go home}.
    
    I was \underline{happy to see them again}.
    
    He was \underline{powerless to prevent it}.
    
    I was almost \underline{ashamed to tell her}.
    
    The path was \underline{easy to follow}.
\end{example}
Note that the subject of the main clause is also the subject of the \textit{to}-infinitive clause.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.53} When adjectives that refer to someone's beliefs or feelings are used after a linking verb, they are often followed by a \textbf{\textit{that}-clause} (see paragraphs \textbf{8.119--8.121}).

The subject of the \textit{that}-clause is not always the same as the subject of the main clause, so you need to specify it.
\begin{example}
    She was \underline{sure that} he meant it.
    
    He was \underline{frightened that} something terrible might be said.
    
    I'm \underline{aware that} I reached a rather large audience through the book.
\end{example}
note that the word \textit{that} is not always used in a \textit{that}-clause.
\begin{example}
    They were \underline{sure} she had been born in the city.
\end{example}
Here is a list of common adjectives often followed by a \textit{that}-clause:
\begin{example}
    afraid, angry, anxious, aware, certain, confident, frightened, glad, happy, pleased, proud, sad, sorry, sure, surprised, unaware, upset, worried
\end{example}
Note that all of these adjectives except \textit{angry, aware, unaware, upset}, and \textit{worried} can also be followed by a \textit{to}-infinitive.
\begin{example}
    I was \underline{afraid that she might not be able bear the strain}.
    
    Don't be \underline{afraid to ask questions}.
    
    She was \underline{surprised that I knew about it}.
    
    The twins were very \underline{surprised to see Ralph}.
\end{example}

\subsection{Position of adjectives in noun phrases}
\noindent\fbox{2.54} When you use more than 1 adjective in a noun phrase, the usual order for the adjective is: qualitative adjectives, followed by color adjectives, followed by classifying adjectives.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{little white wooden} house.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{pretty black lacy} dresses.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{large circular} pool of water.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{beautiful pink} suit.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{rapid technological} advance.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{nice red} apple.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{black triangular} fin.
\end{example}
This order is nearly always followed in English.

Occasionally however, when you want to focus on a particular characteristic of the person or thing you are describing, you can vary this order, especially when 1 of the adjectives refers to color or size.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{square black} hole.
\end{example}
Note that you sometimes put a common or \textit{and} between adjectives.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{8.180--8.186} and paragraph \textbf{8.201}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the \underline{long, low} caravan.
    
    It was a \underline{long and tedious} business.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.55} \textbf{Comparatives} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.103--2.111}) and \textbf{superlatives} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.112--2.122}) normally come in front of all other adjectives in a noun phrase.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{better} parental control.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{highest} monthly figures on record.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.56} When a noun phrase contains both an adjective and a noun modifier (see paragraphs \textbf{2.169--2.174}) the adjective is placed in front of the noun modifier.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the booming European \underline{car} industry.
    
    $\ldots$ the world's biggest and most prestigious \underline{book} fair.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.57} When you use 2 adjectives after a linking verb, you use a conjunction, usually \textit{and}, to link them.

If you use more than 2 adjectives, you usually put a conjunction e.g. \textit{and} between the last 2 adjectives and commas between the others.

This is fully explained in paragraphs \textbf{8.180--8.186} and paragraph \textbf{8.201}.
\begin{example}
    The room was \underline{large and square}.
    
    We felt \underline{hot, tired, and thirsty}.
\end{example}
Note that you put the adjectives in the order that you think is the most important.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.58} There are a few adjectives that are usually or always used after a noun.

Here is a list showing the different groups of adjectives used after a noun:
\begin{example}
    designate, elect, galore, incarnate, manqu\'e
    
    broad, deep, high, long, old, tall, thick, wide
    
    concerned, involved, present, proper, responsible
    
    affected, available, required, suggested
\end{example}
%
\textbf{[i] Usage note.}

%
\noindent\fbox{2.59} The adjectives \textit{designate, elect, galore, incarnate}, and \textit{manqu\'e} are only used immediately after a noun.
\begin{example}
    She was now president \textit{elect}.
    
    There are empty houses \underline{galore}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.60} The adjectives \textit{broad, deep, high, long, old, tall, thick}, and \textit{wide} are used immediately after measurement nouns when giving the size, duration, or age of a thing or person.

This use is fully explained in paragraph \textbf{2.253}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ 6 feet \underline{tall}.
    
    $\ldots$ 3 meters \underline{wide}.
    
    $\ldots$ 25 years \underline{old}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.61} The adjectives \textit{concerned, involved, present, responsible}, and \textit{proper} have different meanings depending on whether you put them in front of a noun or immediately after one.

E.g., \textit{the concerned mother} describes a mother who is anxious, but \textit{the mother concerned} simply refers to a mother who has just been mentioned.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the approval of interested and \underline{concerned} parents.
    
    The idea needs to come from the individuals \underline{concerned}.
    
    All this became a very \underline{involved} process.
    
    He knew all of the people \underline{involved}.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{present} international situation.
    
    Of the 18 people \underline{present}, I know only one.
    
    $\ldots$ parents trying to act in a \underline{responsible} manner.
    
    $\ldots$ the person \underline{responsible} for his death.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{proper} training in how to teach.
    
    $\ldots$ the 1st round \underline{proper} of the FA Cup.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.62} The adjectives \textit{affected, available, required}, and \textit{suggested} can be used in front of a noun or after a noun without any change in meaning.
\begin{example}
    Newspaper were the only \underline{available} source of information.
    
    $\ldots$ the number of teachers \underline{available}.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{required} changes.
    
    You're way below the standard \underline{required}.
    
    $\ldots$ the cost of the \underline{suggested} improvements.
    
    The proposals \underline{suggested} are derived from successful experiments.
    
    Aside from the \underline{affected} child, the doctor checks every other member of the household.
    
    $\ldots$ the proportion of the population \underline{affected}.
\end{example}

\subsection{Special forms: \textit{-ing} adjectives}
\noindent\fbox{2.63} There are many adjectives ending in \textit{-ing}.

Most of them are related in form to the \textbf{\textit{-ing} participles} of verbs.

In this grammar they are called \textbf{\textit{-ing} adjectives}.
\begin{example}
    He was an amiable, \underline{amusing} fellow.
    
    He had been up all night attending a \underline{dying} man.
\end{example}
The \textbf{\textit{-ing} form} is explained in the Reference section.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.64} 1 group of \textit{-ing} adjectives describe the effect that something has on your feelings and ideas, or on the feelings and ideas of people in general.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ an \underline{alarming} increase in burglaries.
    
    A \underline{surprising} number of men do not marry.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{charming} house on the outskirts of the town.
    
    $\ldots$ a warm \underline{welcoming} smile.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.65} These adjectives are normally \textbf{qualitative adjectives}.

This means that they can be used with a \textbf{submodifying adverb} (a word like \textit{very} or \textit{rather}), and have comparatives and superlatives.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{very convincing} example.
    
    There is nothing \underline{very surprising} in this.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{very exciting} idea.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{really pleasing} evening at the theater.
    
    When Bernard moans he's much \underline{more convincing}.
    
    $\ldots$ 1 of \underline{the most boring} books I've ever read.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.66} They can be used in front of a noun or after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    They can still show \underline{amazing} loyalty to their parents.
    
    It's \underline{amazing} what they can do.
    
    $\ldots$ the most \underline{terrifying} tale ever written.
    
    The present situation is \underline{terrifying}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.67} These \textit{-ing} adjectives have a related transitive verb that you use to describe the way someone is affected by something.

E.g., if you speak of \textit{an alarming increase}, you mean that the increase alarms you.

If you speak of \textit{a surprising number}, you meant that the number surprises you.

%
Here is a list of \textit{-ing} adjectives that describe an effect and that have a similar meaning to the usual meaning of the related verb:
\begin{example}
    alarming, amazing, amusing, annoying, appalling, astonishing, astounding, bewildering, boring, challenging, charming, compelling, confusing, convincing, demeaning, depressing, devastating, disappointing, disgusting, distracting, distressing, disturbing, embarrassing, enchanting, encouraging, entertaining, exciting, frightening, harassing, humiliating, infuriating, inspiring, interesting, intimidating, intriguing, menacing, misleading, mocking, overwhelming, pleasing, refreshing, relaxing, rewarding, satisfying, shocking, sickening, starling, surprising, tempting, terrifying, threatening, thrilling, tiring, welcoming, worrying
\end{example}
Transitive verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.14--3.25}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.68} The other main group of \textit{-ing} adjectives are used to describe a process or state that continues over a period of time.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ her \underline{growing} band of supporters.
    
    Oil and gas drillers are doing a \underline{booming} business.
    
    $\ldots$ a life of \underline{increasing} labor and \underline{decreasing} leisure.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.69} These adjectives are \textbf{classifying adjectives}, so they are not used with words like \textit{very} and \textit{rather}.

However, adjectives used to identify a process are often modified by adverbs that describe the speed with which the process happens.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{fast diminishing} degree of personal freedom.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{rapidly rising} productivity.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.70} These \textit{-ing} adjectives have related intransitive verbs.

%
Here is a list of \textit{-ing} adjectives that describe a continuing process or state and that have a similar meaning to the usual meaning of the related verb:
\begin{example}
    ageing, ailing, bleeding, booming, bursting, decreasing, diminishing, dwindling, dying, existing, increasing, living, prevailing, recurring, reigning, remaining, resounding, rising, ruling
\end{example}
Intransitive verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.8--3.13}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.71} These \textit{-ing} adjectives are only used in front of a noun, so when \textit{-ing} forms of intransitive verbs appear after the verb \textit{be} they are actually part of a progressive form.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.72} [$\star$] In English, you can make most verbs into adjectives by adding \textit{-ing} to the verb and putting it in front of the noun, to say that someone or something is doing.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{walking} figure.
    
    $\ldots$ FIFA, world football's \underline{ruling} body.
    
    $\ldots$ bands performing in front of \underline{screaming} crowds.
    
    $\ldots$ 2 years of \underline{falling} employment.
    
    $\ldots$ a tremendous noise of \underline{smashing} glass.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.73} Most of the \textit{-ing} adjectives talked about so far are related to verbs.

Sometimes however, \textit{-ing} adjectives are not related to verbs at all.

E.g., there is no verb \textit{to neighbor}.
\begin{example}
    Whole families came from \underline{neighboring} villages.
\end{example}
Here is a list of \textit{-ing} adjectives that are not related to verbs:
\begin{example}
    appetizing, balding, cunning, enterprising, excruciating, impending, neighboring, scathing, unwitting
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.74} Sometimes, an \textit{-ing} adjectives is related to an uncommon use of a verb, or appears to be related to a verb but is not related exactly to any current use.

E.g., the verb \textit{haunt} is most commonly used in connection with ghosts, but the adjective \textit{haunting} is more often used to talk about such things as songs and memories.

A \textit{haunting tune} is a tune you cannot forget.

%
Here is a list of qualitative \textit{-ing} adjectives that are not related to a common transitive use of a verb:
\begin{example}
    becoming, bracing, cutting, dashing, disarming, engaging, fetching, halting, haunting, moving, penetrating, piercing, pressing, promising, rambling, ravishing, retiring, revolting, searching, taxing, trying
\end{example}
Here is a list of classifying \textit{-ing} adjectives that are not related to a common intransitive use of a verb:
\begin{example}
    acting, driving, floating, gathering, going, leading, missing, running
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.75} Some adjectives are derived from a verb and a prefix.

E.g., \textit{outgoing} is derived from the verb \textit{go} and the prefix \textit{out-}.

There is no verb \textit{to outgo}.
\begin{example}
    Wouldn't that cause a delay in \underline{outgoing} mail?
\end{example}
here is a list of \textit{-ing} adjectives derived from a verb and a prefix:
\begin{example}
    forthcoming, incoming, oncoming, ongoing, outgoing, outstanding, overarching, overbearing, uplifting, upstanding
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.76} A small group of \textit{-ing} adjectives are used in informal spoken English for emphasis, usually to express disapproval.

This use is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.41--2.42}.

%
Some \textbf{compound adjectives} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.94--2.102}) end in \textit{-ing}.

\subsection{Special forms: \textit{-ed} adjectives}
\noindent\fbox{2.77} A large number of English adjectives end in \textit{-ed}.

Many of them have the same form as the \textbf{\textit{-ed} participle} of a verb.

Others are formed by adding \textit{-ed} to a noun.

Others are not closely related to any other words.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{disappointed} man.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{bearded} man.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{sophisticated} electronic devices.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.78} Adjectives with the same form as irregular \textbf{\textit{-ed} participles} (see the Reference section) that do not end in \textit{-ed} are also included here as \textit{-ed} adjectives.
\begin{example}
    Was it a \underline{broken} bone, a \underline{torn} ligament, or what?
\end{example}
The \textit{-ed} participles of some \textbf{phrasal verbs} (see paragraphs \textbf{3.83--3.116}) can also be used as adjectives.

When they are used in front of a noun, the 2 parts of the phrasal verb are usually written with a hyphen between them.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the \underline{built-up} urban mass of the city.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.79} Most \textit{-ed} adjectives are related to a transitive verb and have a passive meaning.

They show that something has happened or is happening to the thing being described.

E.g., \textit{a frightened person} is a person who has been frightened by something.

\textit{A known criminal} is a criminal who is known by the police.
\begin{example}
    We have a long list of \underline{satisfied} customers.
    
    We cannot refuse to teach children the \underline{required} subjects.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.80} \textit{-ed} adjectives that refer to a person's mental or emotional reaction to something are generally qualitative.
\begin{example}
    He was a \underline{worried} old man.
    
    $\ldots$ a bored old woman.
    
    $\ldots$ an \underline{interested} student.
\end{example}
These adjectives can be modified by words e.g. \textit{very} and \textit{extremely}, just like other qualitative adjectives (see paragraphs \textbf{2.140--2.156}).

%
\noindent\fbox{2.81} Like other adjectives used for talking about feelings, these adjectives are often used to describe the expression, voice, or manner or the person affected, instead of referring directly to that person.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ her big blue \underline{frightened} eyes.
    
    She could hear his \underline{agitated} voice.
    
    Barry gave him a \underline{worried} look.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.82} Here is a list of qualitative \textit{-ed} adjectives that have a similar meaning to the most common meaning of the related verb:
\begin{example}
    agitated, alarmed, amused, appalled, astonished, bored, confused, contented, delighted, depressed, deprived, disappointed, disgusted, disillusioned, distressed, embarrassed, excited, frightened, inhibited, interested, pleased, preoccupied, puzzled, satisfied, shocked, surprised, tired, troubled, worried
\end{example}
Here is a list of qualitative \textit{-ed} adjectives that do not have a similar meaning to the usual meaning of the related verb:
\begin{example}
    animated, attached, concerned, determined, disposed, disturbed, guarded, hurt, inclined, mixed, strained
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.83} Many other \textit{-ed} adjectives are used for classifying, and so cannot be used with words like \textit{very} and \textit{rather}.

E.g., \textit{a furnished apartment} is 1 type of apartment, contrasting with \textit{an apartment without furniture}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{furnished} apartment.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{painted} wooden bowl.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{closed} bedroom door.
\end{example}
Most adjectives that refer to physical distinctions are classifying adjectives.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.84} Here is a list of classifying \textit{-ed} adjectives that have a similar meaning to the most common meaning of the related verb:
\begin{example}
    abandoned, armed, blocked, boiled, broken, canned, classified, closed, concentrated, condemned, cooked, divided, drawn, dried, established, fixed, furnished, haunted, hidden, improved, infected, integrated, known, licensed, loaded, paid, painted, processed, reduced, required, torn, trained, united, wasted
\end{example} 
Here is list of \textit{-ed} classifying adjectives that have a different meaning from the most common meaning of the related verb:
\begin{example}
    advanced, marked, noted, pointed, spotted, veiled
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.85} Classifying \textit{-ed} adjectives cannot normally be modified with words like \textit{quite} and \textit{very}.

However, an \textbf{adverb of manner}, (see paragraphs \textbf{6.36--6.44}) or an \textbf{adverb of degree}, (see paragraphs \textbf{6.45--6.52}) is often used before an \textit{-ed} adjective.

%
E.g., \textit{a pleasantly furnished room} is a room that has been furnished with pleasant furniture.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{pleasantly furnished} rooms.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{well-known} novelist.
\end{example} 
%
\noindent\fbox{2.86} Some \textit{-ed} adjectives are not often used on their own, and an adverb is necessary to complete the sense.

You do not usually talk about \textit{dressed people}, but you can say that they are \textit{well dressed} or \textit{smartly dressed} e.g.

The \textit{-ed} adjectives in the following examples nearly always have an adverb in front of them.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{cautiously worded} statement.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{impeccably dressed} men.
    
    It was a \underline{richly deserved} honor.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{superbly cut} clothes.
    
    $\ldots$ the existence of a \underline{highly developed} national press.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{well organized} campaign.
    
    $\ldots$ a tall, \underline{powerfully built} man.
    
    She gazed down at his \underline{perfectly formed} little face.
\end{example}
Note that combinations like this are sometimes hyphenated, making them \textbf{compound adjectives}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{well-equipped} army.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.87} A few \textit{-ed} adjectives are related to the \textit{-ed} participle of intransitive verbs and have an active meaning, not a passive meaning.

E.g., \textit{a fallen tree} is a tree that has fallen.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{capsized} ship.
    
    She is the daughter of a \underline{retired} army officer.
    
    $\ldots$ an \underline{escaped} prisoner.
\end{example}
Here is a list of \textit{-ed} adjectives with an active meaning:
\begin{example}
    accumulated, dated, escaped, faded, fallen, retired, swollen, wilted
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.88} Most \textit{-ed} adjectives can be used both in front of a noun and after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    The \underline{worried} authorities decided to play safe.
    
    My husband was \underline{worried}.
\end{example}
A small number of \textit{-ed} adjectives are normally only used after a linking verb.

Often, they are followed by a preposition, a \textit{to}-infinitive, or a \textit{that}-clause.
\begin{example}
    I was \underline{thrilled} by the exhibition.
    
    The Brazilians are \underline{pleased} with the results.
    
    $\ldots$ food \underline{destined} for areas of south Sudan.
    
    He was always \underline{prepared} to account for his actions.
\end{example}
Here is a list of \textit{-ed} adjectives often used after a linking verb, with or without a phrase or clause after them:
\begin{example}
    convinced, delighted, interested, intimidated, intrigued, involved, pleased, prepared, scared, thrilled, tired, touched
\end{example}
Here is a list of \textit{-ed} adjectives normally used after a linking verb with a phrase or clause after them:
\begin{example}
    agreed, destined, dressed, finished, lost, prepared, shut, stuck
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.89} [$\star$] The \textit{-ed} participle of almost any transitive verb can be used as an adjective, though some are more commonly used than others.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ she said, with a \underline{forced} smile.
    
    There was 1 \underline{paid} tutor and 3 volunteer tutors.
    
    The \underline{recovered} animals will be released.
    
    $\ldots$ the final \underline{corrected} version.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.90} $\star$ Some \textit{-ed} adjectives are formed from nouns.

E.g., if a living thing has wings, you can describe it as \textit{winged}.

Inf someone has skills, you can describe them as \textit{skilled}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{winged} angels.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{skilled} engineer.
    
    She was dressed in black and carried a black \underline{beaded} purse.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{armored} cars.
    
    $\ldots$ the education of \underline{gifted} children.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.91} Here is a list of \textit{-ed} adjectives formed from nouns:
\begin{example}
    armored, barbed, beaded, bearded, detailed, flowered, freckled, gifted, gloved, hooded, mannered, pointed, principled, salaried, skilled, spotted, striped, turbaned, walled, winged
\end{example}
\textit{-ed} adjectives formed from nouns are commonly used as the 2nd part of \textbf{compound adjectives} (see paragraph \textbf{2.94--2.102}) e..g \textit{grey-haired} and \textit{open-minded}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.92} There are also some \textit{-ed} adjectives in regular use that are not related to verbs or nouns in the ways described above.

E.g., there are no words \textit{parch} or \textit{belove}.

There is a noun \textit{concert}, but the adjective \textit{concerted} does not mean \textit{having a concert}.
\begin{example}
    He climbed up the dry \underline{parched} grass to the terrace steps.
    
    $\ldots$ a complex and \underline{antiquated} system of taxation.
    
    $\ldots$ attempts to mount a \underline{concerted} campaign.
    
    $\ldots$ the purchase of expensive \underline{sophisticated} equipment.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.93} Here is a list of \textit{-ed} adjectives that are not related to verbs or nouns:
\begin{example}
    antiquated, ashamed, assorted, beloved, bloated, concerted, crazed, deceased, doomed, indebted, parched, rugged, sophisticated, tinned
\end{example}

\subsection{Compound adjectives}
\noindent\fbox{2.94} \textbf{Compound adjectives} are made up of 2 or more words, usually written with hyphens between them.

They may be qualitative, classifying, or color adjectives.
\begin{example}
    I was in a \underline{light-hearted} mood.
    
    She was dressed in a \underline{bottle-green} party dress.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{built-up} urban mass of the city.
    
    $\ldots$ an \underline{air-conditioned} restaurant.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{good-looking} girl.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{1-way} traffic.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{part-time} job.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.95} These are the most common and least restricted patterns for forming compound adjectives:
\begin{itemize}
    \item adjective or number plus noun plus \textit{-ed}, e.g. \textit{grey-haired} and \textit{1-sided}
    \item adjective or adverb plus \textit{-ed} participle, e.g. \textit{low-paid} and \textit{well-behaved}
    \item adjective, adverb, or noun plus \textit{-ing} participle, e.g. \textit{good-looking, long-lasting} and \textit{man-eating}.
\end{itemize}
Note that compound adjectives describe simple concepts: a \textit{good-looking} person looks good, and a \textit{man-eating} beast eats humans.

More complex descriptions in English need to be given using a following phrase or clause.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.96} These are less common and more restricted patterns for forming compound adjectives:
\begin{itemize}
    \item noun plus \textit{-ed} participle, e.g. \textit{tongue-tied} and \textit{wind-swept}
    \item noun plus adjective, e.g. \textit{accident-prone, trouble-free}
    \item adjective plus noun, e.g. \textit{deep-sea, present day}
    \item \textit{-ed} participle plus adverb, e.g. \textit{run-down, cast-off}
    \item number plus singular countable noun, e.g. \textit{5-page, 4-door}
\end{itemize}
Note that compound adjectives formed according to the last of these patterns are always used in front of a noun.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.97} Here is a list of compound qualitative adjectives:
\begin{example}
    able-bodied, absent-minded, accident-prone, big-headed, clear-cut, close-fitting, cold-blooded, easy-going, far-fetched, far-reaching, good-looking, good-tempered, hard-up, hard-wearing, ill-advised, kind-hearted, labor saving, laid-back, light-hearted, long-lasting, long-standing, long-suffering, low-cut, low-paid, low-slung, mind-blowing, mouth-watering, muddle-headed, narrow-minded, nice-looking, off-color, off-hand, off-putting, old-fashioned, 1-sided, open-minded, run-down, 2nd-class, 2nd-rate, shop-soiled, short-handed, short-lived, short-sighted, short-tempered, slow-witted, smooth-talking, soft-hearted, starry-eyed, strong-minded, stuck-up, sun-tanned, swollen-headed, tender-hearted, thick-skinned, tongue-tied, top-heavy, trouble-free, 2-edged, 2-faced, warm-hearted, well-balanced, well-behaved, well-dressed, well-known, well-off, wind-blown, wordly-wise, wrong-headed
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.98} Here is a list of compound classifying adjectives:
\begin{example}
    air-conditioned, all-out, all-powerful, audio-visual, blue-blooded, bow-legged, brand-new, breast-fed, broken-down, broken-hearted, built-up, bullet-proof, burnt-out, cast-off, clean-shaven, cross-Channel, cross-country, cut-price, deep-sea, deep-seated, double-barreled, double-breasted, drip-dry, drive-in, duty-bound, duty-free, empty-handed, face-saving, far-flung, 1st-class, free-range, free-standing, freeze-dried, front-page, full-blown, full-face, full-grown, full-length, full-scale, gilt-edged, grey-haired, half-price, half-yearly, hand-picked, high-heeled, home-made, ice-cold, interest-free, knee-deep, last-minute, late-night, lead-free, left-handed, lift-size, long-distance, long-lost, long-range, loose-leaf, made-up, man-eating, mass-produced, middle-aged, never-ending, north-east, north-west, nuclear-free, odds-on, off-guard, off-peak, 1-way, open-ended, open-mouthed, panic-stricken, part-time, present-day, purpose-built, ready-made, record-breaking, red-brick, remote-controlled, right-angled, right-handed, 2nd-class, 2nd-hand, see-through, silver-plated, single-handed, so-called, so-so, south-east, south-west, strong-arm, tax-free, tone-deaf, top-secret, unheard-of, wide-awake, world-famous, worn-out, year-long
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.99} Here is a list of compound color adjectives:
\begin{example}
    blood-red, blue-black, bottle-green, dove-grey, electric-blue, flesh-colored, ice-blue, iron-grey, jet-black, lime-green, navy-blue, nut-brown, off-white, pea-green, pearl-grey, royal-blue, shocking-pink, sky-blue, snow-white
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.100} A few compound adjectives are made up of more than 2 words.

Compounds of 3 or more words are often written with hyphens when they are used in front of nouns, and without hyphens when they are used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the \underline{day-to-day} chores of life.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{down-to-earth} approach.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{free-and-easy} relationship.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{life-and-death} decisions.
    
    $\ldots$ a trip to an \underline{out-of-the-way} resort.
    
    Their act is \underline{out of date}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.101} Some compound adjectives seem rather odd because they contain words that are never used as single words on their own, e.g. \textit{namby-pamby, higgledy-piggledy}, and \textit{topsy-turvy}.

Words like these are usually informal.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ all that \underline{artsy-craftsy} spiritualism.
    
    $\ldots$ his \underline{la-di-da} family.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.102} Some compound adjectives are borrowed from foreign languages, especially from French and Latin.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the arguments once used to defend \underline{laissez-faire} economics.
    
    $\ldots$ their present \underline{per capita} fuel consumption.
    
    In the commercial theater, almost every production is \underline{ad hoc}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of compound adjectives borrowed from other languages:
\begin{example}
    \`a la mode, a posteriori, a priori, ad hoc, ad lib, au fait, avant-garde, bona fide, compos mentis, cordon bleu, de facto, de jure, de luxe, de rigueur, de trop, ex gratia, hors de combat, infra dig, laissez-faire, non compas mentis, per capita, prima facie, pro rata, sub judice
\end{example}

\section{Comparing things: comparatives}
\noindent\fbox{2.103} You can describe something by saying that it has more of a quality than something else.

You do this by using \textbf{comparative adjectives}.

Only qualitative adjectives usually have comparatives, but a few color adjectives also have them.

Comparatives normally consist of the usual form of the adjective with either \textit{-er} added to the end, as in \textit{harder} and \textit{smaller}, or \textit{more} placed in front, as in \textit{more interesting} and \textit{more flexible}.

%
Note that \textit{good} and \textit{bad} have the irregular comparative forms \textit{better} and \textit{worse}.

%
The patterns for forming regular and irregular comparatives are explained in the Reference section.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.104} Comparatives can be used as \textbf{modifiers} in front of a noun.
\begin{example}
    The family moved to a \underline{smaller} home.
    
    He dreams of a \underline{better, more exciting} life.
    
    A \underline{harder} mattress often helps with black injuries.
\end{example}
Note that comparatives can also be used as modifiers in front of \textit{one}.
\begin{example}
    An understanding of this reality provokes a \underline{better} one.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.105} Comparatives can also be used after a \textbf{linking verb}.
\begin{example}
    The ball soaked up water and became \underline{heavier}.
    
    His breath became \underline{quieter}.
    
    We need to be \underline{more flexible}.
\end{example}
The use of adjectives after linking verbs is explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.132--3.137}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.106} Comparatives are often followed by \textit{than} when you want to specify what the other thing involved in the comparison is.

You say exactly what you are comparing by using 1 of a number of structures after \textit{than}.

%
These structures can be
\begin{example}
    noun phrases
    \begin{example}
        Charlie was \underline{more honest than his predecessor}.
        
        $\ldots$ an area \underline{bigger than Mexico}.
    \end{example}
    Note that when \textit{than} is followed by a pronoun on its own, the pronoun must be an object pronouns e.g. \textit{me, him}, or \textit{her}.
    \begin{example}
        My brother is \underline{younger than me}.
        
        Lamin was \underline{shorter than her}.
    \end{example}
    \item phrases that start with a preposition
    \begin{example}
        The changes will be even \underline{more striking} in the case of teaching \underline{than in medicine}.
        
        The odds of surviving childhood in New York City are \underline{worse than in some 3rd World countries}.
    \end{example}
    \item clauses
    \begin{example}
        I would have done a \underline{better} job \underline{than he did}.
        
        I was a \underline{better} writer \underline{than he was}.
        
        He's \underline{taller than I am}.
    \end{example}
\end{example}
Note that when a comparative is not followed by a \textit{than} phrase, the other thing in the comparison should be obvious.

E.g., if someone says \textit{Could I have a bigger one, please?} they are likely to be holding the item that they think is too small.
\begin{example}
    A mattress would be better.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.107} If you choose a phrase or clause beginning with \textit{than} when you are using a comparative in front of a noun, you usually put the phrase or clause after the whole noun phrase, not directly after the comparative.
\begin{example}
    The world is a \underline{more dangerous} place \underline{than it was}.
    
    Willy owned a \underline{larger} collection of books \underline{than anyone else I have ever met}.
\end{example}
A comparative can also come immediately after a noun, but only when it is followed by \textit{than} and a noun phrase.
\begin{example}
    We've got a rat \underline{bigger than} a cat living in our roof.
    
    $\ldots$ packs of cards \underline{larger than} he was used to.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.108} \textit{More} is sometimes used in front of a whole noun phrase to show that something has more of the qualities of 1 thing than another, or is 1 thing rather than being another.
\begin{example}
    Music is \underline{more} a way of life \underline{than} an interest.
    
    This is \underline{more} a war movie \underline{than} a western.
\end{example}
Note that \textit{more than} is used before adjectives for emphasis.
\begin{example}
    Their life may be horribly full, but they are \underline{more than satisfied}.
    
    You would be \underline{more than welcome}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.109} Comparative adjectives are sometimes used as noun-type words in fairly formal English.

In such phrases, you put \textit{the} in front of it, and follow it with \textit{of} and a noun phrase that refers to the 2 things being compared.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{the shorter of the 2 lines}.
    
    Dorothea was \underline{the more beautiful of the 2}.
    
    There are 2 windmills, \underline{the larger of which} stands a hundred feet high.
\end{example}
If it is clear what you are talking about, you can omit \textit{of} and the following noun phrase.
\begin{example}
    Notice to quit must cover the rental period or 4 weeks, whichever is \underline{the longer}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.110} The form that is used to say that something does not have as much of a quality as something else is \textit{less} followed by an adjective.
\begin{example}
    The answer had been \underline{less truthful} than his own.
\end{example}
You can also use \textit{less} and an adjective to say that something does not have as much of a quality as it had before.
\begin{example}
    As the days went by, Sita became \underline{less anxious}.
\end{example}
Note that \underline{less than} is used before adjectives to express a negative idea.
\begin{example}
    It would have been \underline{less than fair}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.111} You show that 1 amount of a quality or thing is linked to another amount by using 2 contrasted comparatives preceded by \textit{the}.
\begin{example}
    \underline{The smaller} it is, \underline{the cheaper} it is to post.
    
    \underline{The more militant} we became, \underline{the less confident} she became.
    
    \underline{The larger} the organization, \underline{the less scope} there is for decision.
\end{example}

\section{Comparing things: superlatives}
\noindent\fbox{2.112} Another way of describing something is to say that it has more of a quality than anything else of its kind.

You do this by using a \textbf{superlative adjective}.

Only qualitative adjectives usually have superlatives, but a few color adjectives also have them.

Superlatives normally consist of either \textit{-est} added to the end of an adjective and \textit{the} placed in front of it, as in \textit{the hardest} and \textit{the smallest}, or of \textit{the most} placed in front of the adjective, as in \textit{the most interesting} and \textit{the most flexible}.

%
Note that \textit{good} and \textit{bad} have the irregular superlative forms \textit{the best} and \textit{the worst}.

%
The patterns for forming regular and irregular superlatives of adjectives are explained in the Reference section.

%
Note that superlative adjectives are nearly always preceded by \textit{the}, because you are talking about something definite.

Occasionally, when superlatives are used after a linking verb, \textit{the} is omitted (see paragraph \textbf{2.117}).

%
\noindent\fbox{2.113} [!] Adjectives with \textit{most} in front of them are not always superlatives.

\textit{Most} can also mean \textit{very}.
\begin{example}
    This book was \underline{most interesting}.
    
    My grandfather was a \underline{most extraordinary} man.
\end{example}
Words like \textit{very} and \textit{rather} are called \textbf{submodifying adverbs}.

These are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.140--2.156}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.114} Superlatives can be used as \textbf{modifiers} in front of a noun.
\begin{example}
    He was \underline{the cleverest} man I ever knew.
    
    It was \underline{the most exciting} summer of their lives.
    
    She came out of \underline{the thickest} part of the crowd.
    
    Now we come to \underline{the most important} thing.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{the oldest} rock paintings in North America.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{the most eminent} scientists in Britain.
\end{example}
Note that superlatives are also used as modifiers in front of \textit{one}.
\begin{example}
    No one ever used \underline{the smallest} one.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.115} Superlatives are also used after a \textbf{linking verb}.
\begin{example}
    He was \underline{the youngest}.
    
    The sergeant was \underline{the tallest}.
\end{example}
The use of adjectives after linking verbs is explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.132--3.137}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.116} You can use a superlative on its own if it is clear what is being compared.

E.g., if you say \textit{Paul was the tallest}, you are referring to a group of people that has already been identified.

%
If you need to refer to the point of the comparison, you use a phrase or clause that consists of
\begin{itemize}
    \item phrases that start with a preposition, usually \textit{in} or \textit{of}
    \begin{example}
