        Henry was \underline{the biggest of them}.
        
        The 3rd requirement is \underline{the most important of all}.
        
        These cakes are probably \underline{the best in the world}.
    \end{example}
    \item a relative clause
    \begin{example}
        It's \underline{the best} I'm likely to get.
        
        The waiting room was \underline{the worst} I had seen.
    \end{example}
\end{itemize}
Note that if the superlative is placed in front of a noun, the relative clause comes after the noun.
\begin{example}
    That's \underline{the most convincing} answer that you've given me.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.117} [i] You usually put \textit{the} in front of the superlative, but you can occasionally omit it, especially in informal speech or writing.
\begin{example}
    Wool and cotton blankets are generally \underline{cheapest}.
    
    It can be used by whoever is \underline{closest}.
\end{example}
However, you cannot omit \textit{the} when the superlative is followed by \textit{of} or another structure showing what group of things you are comparing.

So, e.g., you can say \textit{Amanda was the youngest of our group} or \textit{Amanda was the youngest} or \textit{Amanda was youngest}, but you cannot say \textit{Amanda was youngest of our group}.

%
You can sometimes use the possessive form of a noun or a possessive determiner instead of \textit{the} in front of a superlative.

Often the possessive form of a noun is used instead of a phrase beginning with a preposition.

E.g., you can say \textit{Britain's oldest man} instead of \textit{the oldest man in Britain}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{the world's most popular} cheese.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{my newest} assistant.
\end{example}
The possessive form of nouns is explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.211-1.222}, and possessive determiners are explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.194--1.210}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.118} A superlative is sometimes accompanied by another adjective ending in \textit{-able} or \textit{-ible}.

This 2nd adjective can be placed either between the superlative and the noun or after the noun.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{the narrowest imaginable} range of interests.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{the most beautiful} scenery \underline{imaginable}.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{the longest possible} gap.
    
    I say that in \underline{the nicest} way \underline{possible}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.119} Superlative adjectives are sometimes used like nouns in fairly formal English.

When you use a superlative adjective in this way, you put \textit{the} in front of it, and follow it with \textit{of} and a noun or pronoun that refers to the things being compared.

When superlative adjectives are used in this way they can refer to 1 thing or to more than one.
\begin{example}
    They are often too poor to buy or rent even \underline{the cheapest} of houses.
    
    He made several important discoveries. \underline{The most interesting} of these came from an examination of an old manuscript.
\end{example}
If it is clear what you are talking about, you can omit \textit{of} and the following noun phrase.
\begin{example}
    There are 3 types of ant-eater. \underline{The smallest} lives entirely in trees.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.120} [i] In informal speech, people often use a superlative rather than a comparative when they are talking about 2 things.

E.g., someone might say \textit{The train is quickest} rather than \textit{The train is quicker} when comparing a train service with a bus service.

However, some people think that it is better to use superlatives only when comparing more than 2 things.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.121} \textbf{Ordinal numbers} are used with superlatives to show that something has more of a quality than nearly all other things of their kind.

E.g., if you say that a mountain is \textit{the 2nd highest mountain}, you mean that it is higher than any other mountain except the highest one.
\begin{example}
    Cancer is \underline{the 2nd biggest} cause of death in Britain.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{the 2nd most important} man in her life, her hairdresser.
    
    It is \underline{Japan's 3rd largest} city.
\end{example}
Ordinal numbers are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.232--2.239}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.122} When you want to show that something has less of a quality than anything else, you use \textit{the least} followed by an adjective.
\begin{example}
    This is \underline{the least popular} branch of medicine.
\end{example}
Similarly, when you are talking about a group of things that have less of a quality than other things of their kind, you use \textit{the least}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{the least savage} men in the country.
\end{example}

\section{Other ways of comparing things: saying that things are similar}
\noindent\fbox{2.123} Another way of describing things is by saying that something is similar in some way to something else.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.124} If you want to say that a thing or person has as much of a quality as something or someone else, you can use a structure based on the word \textit{as} in front of a qualitative adjective.

Usually this adjective is followed by a phrase or clause that also begins with \textit{as}.

%
This can be
\begin{itemize}
    \item a phrase beginning with the preposition \textit{as}
    \begin{example}
        You're just \underline{as bad as your sister}.
        
        $\ldots$ huge ponds \underline{as big as tennis courts}.
        
        Talking were \underline{as high as ever}.
    \end{example}
    \item a clause introduced by \textit{as}
    \begin{example}
        Conversation was not \underline{as slow as I feared it would be}.
        
        The village gardens aren't \underline{as good as they used to be}.
    \end{example}
\end{itemize}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.125} When this comparative structure is followed by a phrase consisting of \textit{as} and a pronoun on its own, the pronoun must be an object pronoun e.g. \textit{me, him}, or \textit{her}.
\begin{example}
    Jane was not as clever as \underline{him}.
\end{example}
However, when the comparative structure is followed by a clause consisting of \textit{as} and a pronoun that is the subject of a clause, then that pronoun must be a subject pronoun e.g. \textit{I, he} or \textit{she}.
\begin{example}
    They aren't as clever as \underline{they appear to be}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.126} If it is clear what you are comparing something to someone to, you can omit the phrase or clause.
\begin{example}
    Frozen peas are just \underline{as good}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.127} You can also use the \textit{as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} structure to say that something has much more or less of a quality than something else.

You do this by putting an expression e.g. \textit{twice, 3 times, 10 times}, or \textit{half} in front of the 1st \textit{as}.

E.g., if 1 building is 10 meters high and another building is 20 meters high, you can say that the 2nd building is \textit{twice as high as} the 1st building or that the 1st building is \textit{half as high as} the 2nd one.
\begin{example}
    The grass was \underline{twice as tall as in the rest of the field}.
    
    Water is \underline{800 times as dense as air}.
\end{example}
This structure is often used in the same way to refer to qualities that cannot be measured.

E.g., if you want to say that something is much more useful than something else, you can say that the 1st thing is \textit{a hundred times as useful as} the 2nd one.
\begin{example}
    Without this help, rearing our children would be \underline{10 times as hard as it is}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.128} [i] When the \textit{as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} structure is preceded by \textit{not}, it has the same meaning as \textit{less$\ldots$ than}.

E.g., \textit{I am not as tall as George} means the same as \textit{I am less tall than George}.

Some people use \textit{not so$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} instead of \textit{not as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$}
\begin{example}
    The film is \underline{not as good as the book}.
    
    The younger otter is \underline{not so handsome as the old}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.129} Words like \textit{just, quite, nearly} and \textit{almost} can be used in front of this comparative structure, modifying the comparison with their usual meanings.
\begin{example}
    Sunburn can be \underline{just as severe as a heat burn}.
\end{example}
The use of these words in comparison is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.157--2.168}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.130} When you are using the \textit{as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} structure you sometimes put a noun after the adjective and before the following phrase or clause.

This noun must begin with \textit{a} or \textit{an}.

E.g., instead of saying \textit{This knife is as good as that one}, you can say \textit{This is as good a knife as that one}.
\begin{example}
    I'm \underline{as good a cook as} she is.
    
    This was not \underline{as bad a result as} they expected.
\end{example}
Sometimes, instead of using \textit{not} before this structure, you use \textit{not such} followed by \textit{a} or \textit{an}, an adjective, a noun, and \textit{as}.
\begin{example}
    Water is \underline{not such a good conductor as} metal.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.131} Instead of using this \underline{as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} structure you can use expressions e.g. \textit{the height of} and \textit{the size of} to show that something is as big as something else, or bigger or smaller.
\begin{example}
    The tumor was \underline{the size of} a golf ball.
    
    It is roughly \underline{the length of} a man's arm.
\end{example} 
%
\noindent\fbox{2.132} If something has similar qualities or features to something else, instead of using the \textit{as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} comparative structures you can say that the 1st thing is \textit{like} the 2nd one.

You do this by using phrases beginning with \textit{like} after \textbf{linking verbs}/
\begin{example}
    He looked \underline{like} an actor.
    
    That sounds \underline{like} an exaggeration.
    
    The whole thing is like a bad dream.
\end{example}
Here is a list of the linking verbs used with \textit{like}:
\begin{example}
    be, feel, look, seem, smell, sound, taste
\end{example}
When you want to say that 1 thing resembles another, you can use a phrase beginning with \textit{like} after these linking verbs.
\begin{example}
    It was \underline{like a dream}.
    
    Sometimes I feel \underline{like a prisoner} here.
    
    He looked \underline{like a nice man}.
    
    The houses seemed \underline{like mansions}.
    
    You smell \underline{like a tramp!}
    
    It sounded \underline{like a fine idea}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.133} \textit{Like} has the comparative \textit{more like} and \textit{less like}, and the superlative \textit{most like} and \textit{least like}.
\begin{example}
    It made her seem \underline{less like} a child.
    
    Of all his children, she was the one \underline{most like} me.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.134} [i] You can use words like \textit{exactly} and \textit{just} in front of \textit{like}.
\begin{example}
    He looks \underline{just like} a baby.
    
    She looked like a queen, \underline{just exactly like} a queen.
\end{example}
This is explained in paragraph \textbf{2.165}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.135} If you want to say that 1 thing is exactly like something else, you can say that it is \textit{the same as} the other thing.
\begin{example}
    The rich are \underline{the same as} the rest of us.
\end{example}
\textit{The same as} can be followed by a noun phrase, a pronoun, or a clause.
\begin{example}
    24 Spring Terrace was \underline{the same as} all its neighbors.
    
    Her coloring was \underline{the same as} mine.
    
    The furnishings are not exactly \underline{the same as} they were when we lived there.
\end{example}
If 2 or more things are exactly like each other, you can say that they are \textit{the same}.
\begin{example}
    Come and look! They're exactly \underline{the same}.
    
    They both taste \underline{the same}.
\end{example}
You use \textit{the same} when you are comparing people or things with other people or things that you have just mentioned.
\begin{example}
    It looks like a calculator and weights about \underline{the same}.
    
    The message was \underline{the same}.
    
    The end result is \underline{the same}.
\end{example}
Note that you use \textit{the opposite} and \textit{the reverse} in a similar way.
\begin{example}
    The kind of religious thoughts I had were just \underline{the opposite}.
    
    Some people think that a healthy diet is expensive, but in fact \underline{the reverse} is true.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.136} [i] You can use words like \textit{nearly} and \textit{exactly} in front of \textit{the same as} and \textit{the same}.
\begin{example}
    They are \underline{virtually the same as} other single cells.
    
    You 2 look \underline{exactly the same}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of words used in the same way with \textit{the same as} and \textit{the same}:
\begin{example}
    almost, exactly, just, more or less, much, nearly, virtually
\end{example}
These words are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.140--2.168}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.137} You can put a noun e.g. \textit{size, length}, or \textit{color} after \textit{the same}.

E.g., if you want to say that 1 street is as long as another one, you can say that the 1st street is \textit{the same length} as the 2nd one, or that the 2 streets are \textit{the same length}.
\begin{example}
    Its brain was about \underline{the same size} as that of a gorilla.
    
    They were almost \underline{the same height}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.138} The adjectives \textit{alike, comparable, equivalent, identical}, and \textit{similar} are also used to say that 2 or more things are like each other.

You can put the preposition \textit{to} after all of them except \textit{alike} in order to mention the 2nd of the things being compared.
\begin{example}
    They all looked \underline{alike}.
    
    The houses were all \underline{identical}.
    
    Flemish is \underline{similar to} Afrikaans.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.139} When you want to suggest that you are comparing different amounts of a quality, you can use words like \textit{comparatively, relatively}, and \textit{equally}.
\begin{example}
    Psychology's a \underline{comparatively new} subject.
    
    The costs remained \underline{relatively low}.
    
    Her technique was less dramatic than Ann's, but \underline{equally effective}.
    
    He was \underline{extra polite} to his superiors.
\end{example}

\section{Talking about different amounts of a quality}
\noindent\fbox{2.140} When you want to say something more about the quality that an adjective describes, you can use a \textbf{submodifying adverb} e.g. \textit{very} or \textit{rather} with it.

You do this in order to indicate the amount of the quality, or to intensify it.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.141} Because qualitative adjectives are \textbf{gradable}, allowing you to say how much or how little of the quality is relevant, you are more likely to use \textbf{submodifying adverbs} (words like \textit{extremely} or \textit{slightly}) with them than with other types of adjective.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ an \underline{extremely narrow} road.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{highly successful} company.
    
    $\ldots$ in a \underline{slightly different} way.
    
    I was \underline{extraordinarily happy}.
    
    $\ldots$ helping them in a \underline{strongly supportive} way.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{very pretty} girl.
    
    She seems \underline{very pleasant}.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{rather clumsy} person.
    
    His hair was \underline{rather long}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.142} You can use words like \textit{very} and \textit{extremely} with some classifying adjectives (see paragraphs \textbf{2.146--2.148}) and with color adjectives (see paragraph \textbf{2.35}).

Note that most \textit{-ed} adjectives can be modified by words e.g. \textit{very} and \textit{extremely}, just like other qualitative adjectives.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{very frightened} little girl.
    
    $\ldots$ an \underline{extremely disappointed} young man.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.143} You can use many submodifying adverbs like \textit{very} or \textit{extremely} with qualitative adjectives in order to intensify their meaning.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{extremely high} temperatures.
    
    Geoffrey was a \underline{deeply religious} man.
    
    France is \underline{heavily dependent} on foreign trade.
\end{example}
Here is a list of words used to intensify the meaning of adjectives:
\begin{example}
    amazingly, awfully, bitterly, critically, dangerously, deeply, delightfully, disturbingly, dreadfully, eminently, especially, exceedingly, extraordinarily, extremely, fantastically, greatly, heavily, highly, hopelessly, horribly, hugely, impossibly, incredibly, infinitely, notably, particularly, radically, really, remarkably, seriously, strikingly, supremely, surprisingly, suspiciously, terribly, unbelievably, very, violently, vitally, wildly, wonderfully
\end{example}
Note that \textit{very} can be used in front of superlative adjectives when you want to be very emphatic.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.167--2.168}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.144} Many of these submodifying adverbs not only intensify the meaning of the adjective but also allow you to express your opinion about what you are saying.

E.g., if you say that something is \textit{surprisingly large}, you are expressing surprise at how large it is as well as intensifying the meaning of \textit{large}.
\begin{example}
    He has \underline{amazingly long} eyelashes.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{delightfully refreshing} taste.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{shockingly brutal} scene.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{horribly uncomfortable} chair.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{incredibly boring} documents.
\end{example}
However, you use a few of these submodifying adverbs with no other purpose than to intensify the meaning of the adjective.
\begin{example}
    They're \underline{awfully brave}.
    
    The other girls were \underline{dreadfully dull} companions.
\end{example}
Here is a list of words only used to intensify adjectives:
\begin{example}
    awfully, dreadfully, especially, extremely, greatly, highly, really, so, terribly, very
\end{example}
Note that \textit{awfully, dreadfully}, and \textit{terribly} are used in informal language and \textit{highly} is used in very formal language.

%
Note also that \textit{so} is normally only used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    I am \underline{so sorry}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.145} Some submodifying adverbs are used to reduce the effect of qualitative adjectives.
\begin{example}
    The story was \underline{mildly amusing}.
    
    It's \underline{fairly common} feeling.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{moderately rich} people.
    
    $\ldots$ his \underline{rather large} stomach.
    
    My last question is \underline{somewhat personal}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of words used to reduce the effect of an adjective:
\begin{example}
    faintly, fairly, mildly, moderately, pretty, quite, rather, reasonably, slightly, somewhat
\end{example}
Note also that \textit{quite} is normally only used with adjectives that are used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    She was \underline{quite tall}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.146} Some modifying adverbs are used to talk about the extent of the quality that you are describing.

%
Here is a list of words used to talk about the extent of a quality:
\begin{example}
    almost, exclusively, fully, largely, mainly, mostly, nearly, partly, predominantly, primarily, roughly
    
    absolutely, altogether, completely, entirely, perfectly, purely, quite, simply, totally, utterly
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.147} The 1st group in the list above are used almost always just to talk about the extent of a quality.

They are most commonly used with classifying adjectives.
\begin{example}
    It was an \underline{almost} automatic reflex.
    
    $\ldots$ a shop with an \underline{exclusively} female clientele.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{largely} rural south east.
    
    The wolf is now \underline{nearly} extinct.
    
    The reasons for this were \underline{partly} economic and practical, and \underline{partly} political and social.
\end{example}
\textit{Almost} and \textit{nearly} are also used with qualitative adjectives.
\begin{example}
    The club was \underline{almost} empty.
    
    It was \underline{nearly} dark.
\end{example}
Note that \textit{roughly} can be used when you want to say that something is nearly or approximately like something else.
\begin{example}
    West Germany, Japan and Sweden are at \underline{roughly similar} levels of economic development.
\end{example}
Note also that \textit{half} is sometimes used in this way.

E.g., you can describe someone as \textit{half American} if just 1 of their parents was American.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.148} The 2nd group in the list above are used not only to talk about the extent of a quality but also to emphasize the adjective.

They are used with classifying adjectives as well as qualitative adjectives.
\begin{example}
    You're \underline{absolutely right}.
    
    This policy has been \underline{completely unsuccessful}.
    
    Everyone appeared to be \underline{completely unaware} of the fact.
    
    The discussion was \underline{purely theoretical}.
    
    It really is \underline{quite astonishing}.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{totally new} situation.
    
    We lived \underline{totally separate} lives.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{utterly trivial} matters.
\end{example}
Note that \textit{absolutely} is frequently used with qualitative adjectives that express enthusiasm or lack of enthusiasm.

When you use \textit{absolutely} in this way you are emphasizing how strongly you feel about what you are saying.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ an \underline{absolutely absurd} idea.
    
    I think it's \underline{absolutely wonderful}.
    
    The enquiry is \underline{absolutely crucial}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of qualitative adjectives often emphasized by \textit{absolutely}:
\begin{example}
    absurd, awful, brilliant, certain, crucial, enormous, essential, excellent, furious, hilarious, huge, impossible, massive, perfect, ridiculous, splendid, terrible, vital, wonderful
\end{example}
Note also that \textit{completely} and \textit{utterly} can also be used in this way.
\begin{example}
    It is \underline{completely} impossible to imagine such a world.
    
    He began to feel \underline{utterly} miserable.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.149} You can use submodifying adverbs e.g. \textit{adequately, sufficiently}, and \textit{acceptably} when you want to say that someone or something has enough of the quality you are describing.
\begin{example}
    The roof is \underline{adequately insulated}.
    
    We found a bank of snow \underline{sufficiently deep} to dig a cave.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.150} [i] You can also show that you think something is sufficient by using \textit{enough}.

\textit{Enough} always comes after the adjective, and never before it.
\begin{example}
    I was not a \underline{good enough} rider.
    
    It seemed that Henry had not been \underline{careful enough}.
\end{example}
\textit{Enough} can be followed by the preposition \textit{for} to indicate a person involved, or by a \textit{to}-infinitive to indicate a related action.
\begin{example}
    A girl from the factory wasn't \underline{good enough for him}.
    
    If you find that the white wine is not \underline{cold enough for you}, ask for some ice to be put in it.
    
    The children are \underline{old enough to travel to school on their own}.
    
    None of the fruit was \underline{ripe enough to eat}.
\end{example}
Note that when \textit{enough} is used after an adjective, you can use \textit{just} in front of the adjective to show that someone or something has enough of the quality described by the adjective, but no more than that.
\begin{example}
    Some of these creatures are \underline{just large enough} to see with the naked eye.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.151} \textit{Enough} is also a determiner (see paragraphs \textbf{1.223--1.247}).
\begin{example}
    He hasn't had \underline{enough} exercise.
\end{example}
When \textit{enough} is a determiner, it can have a word like \textit{just} or \textit{almost} in front of it.
\begin{example}
    There was \underline{just enough} space for a bed.
    
    I have \underline{almost enough} tokens for 1 book.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.152} If you want to show that you think something you are describing is insufficient, you can use submodifying adverbs e.g. \textit{inadequately, insufficiently}, and \textit{unacceptably}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ people growing up in \underline{insufficiently supportive} families.
    
    Their publications were \underline{inadequately researched}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.153} If you want to say that you think someone or something has too much of a quality, you normally use \textit{too} in front of a qualitative adjective that is used after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    My feet are \underline{too big}.
    
    It was \underline{too hot}.
    
    Dad thought I was \underline{too idealistic}.
\end{example}
You can emphasize \textit{too} by putting \textit{far} in front of it.

In informal English you can also use \textit{way}.
\begin{example}
    The journey was \underline{far too long}.
    
    It was \underline{far too hot} to work in the garden.
    
    The price was \underline{way too high}.
\end{example}
\textit{Too} can be followed by the preposition \textit{for} to indicate a person involved or by a \textit{to}-infinitive to indicate a related action.
\begin{example}
    The shoes were \underline{too big for him}.
    
    He was \underline{too old for that sort of thing}.
    
    She was \underline{too weak to lift me}.
    
    He was \underline{too proud to apologize}.
\end{example}
Note that you do not usually use \textit{too} with an adjective in front of a noun, although you do use \textit{too} in front of the determiners \textit{many, much}, and \textit{few}.
\begin{example}
    There is \underline{too much} chance of error.
    
    \underline{Too few} people nowadays are interested in literature.
    
    You ask \underline{too many} questions, Sam.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.154} [!] \textit{Too} cannot be used instead of \textit{very}.

Rather than saying \textit{I am too happy to meet you}, you must say \textit{I am very happy to meet you}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.155} Other words that indicate too much of a quality are \textit{excessively, overly}, and the prefix \textit{over-}.

These can be used, like \textit{too}, with adjectives that come after a linking verb, but they can also be used with adjectives in front of a noun.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{excessively high} accident rates.
    
    $\ldots$ an intellectual but \underline{over-cautious} man.
    
    They were \underline{overly eager}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.156} [$\star$] As well as adverbs of degree like \textit{excessively} and \textit{insufficiently}, you can use some other types of adverb in front of adjectives to modify their meaning.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the \underline{once elegant} palace.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{permanently muddy} road.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{internationally famous} golfers.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{naturally blonde} hair.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{coolly elegant} furniture.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{purposely expensive} gadgets.
\end{example}
Adverbs are explained in Chap. 6.

\section{Saying things are different}
\noindent\fbox{2.157} When you are using comparative adjectives, you may want to say that something has much more or much less of a quantity than something else.

You do this by adding words like \textit{much} or \textit{a little}.
\begin{example}
    It is a \underline{much better} school than yours.
    
    These creatures are \underline{much less mobile}.
    
    These are \underline{far worse} dangers.
    
    Some children are \underline{a lot more difficult} than others.
\end{example}
You also use these words to say that something has much more or much less of a quality than it had before.
\begin{example}
    He had become \underline{much more nature}.
    
    That's \underline{much less important} than it was.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.158} Some modifying words and phrases are only used when comparative adjectives are being used after linking verbs.
\begin{example}
    You look \underline{a lot better}.
    
    It would be \underline{a good deal easier} if you came to my place.
    
    The journey back was \underline{a great deal more unpleasant} than the outward one had been.
\end{example}
Here is a list of modifying words and phrases used in front of comparative adjectives after a linking verb:
\begin{example}
    a good deal, a great deal, a lot, heaps, lots
\end{example}
Note that \textit{lots} and \textit{heaps} are only used in informal spoken English.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.159} However, other submodifying adverbs can be used with comparative adjectives that are being used either in front of a noun or after a linking verb.
\begin{example}
    They are faced with a \underline{much harder} problem than the rest of us.
    
    The risk from smoking is \underline{much greater} if you have a weak heart.
    
    Computers can be applied to a \underline{far wider} range of tasks.
    
    The delay was \underline{far longer} than they claimed.
\end{example}
Here is a list of submodifying adverbs used with adjectives that are used both in front of a noun and after a linking verb:
\begin{example}
    considerably, far, infinitely, much, vastly, very much
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.160} [i] If you want to say that something has more of a quality than something else that already has a lot of it, you use \textit{even} or \textit{still} before a comparative adjective, or \textit{still} after it.
\begin{example}
    She's \underline{even lazier} than me!
    
    She was \underline{even more possessive} than Rosamund.
    
    I had a \underline{still more recent} report.
    
    The text is actually \underline{worse still}/
\end{example}
Similarly, you use \textit{even} or \textit{still} to say that something has less of a quality than something else that has little of this quality.
\begin{example}
    This did not happen before the war, and is now \underline{even less} likely.
\end{example}
You also use \textit{even} or \textit{still} when comparing the amount of a quality that something has at 1 time with the amount that it has at another.
\begin{example}
    The flight was \underline{even faster} coming back.
    
    They will become \underline{richer still}.
\end{example}
In formal or literary English, \textit{yet} is sometimes used in the same way as \textit{still}.
\begin{example}
    He would have been \underline{yet more alarmed} had she withdrawn.
    
    The planes grow \underline{mightier yet}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.161} You can show that something has an increasing or decreasing amount of a quality by repeating comparative adjectives.

E.g., you can say that something is getting \textit{bigger and bigger, more and more difficult}, or \textit{less and less common}.
\begin{example}
    He's getting \underline{taller and taller}.
    
    $\ldots$ defences that were proving \underline{more and more effective}.
\end{example}
\textit{Increasingly} can be used instead of \textit{more and more} and \textit{decreasingly} instead of \textit{less and less}.
\begin{example}
    I was becoming \underline{increasingly} depressed.
    
    It was the 1st of a number of \underline{increasingly} frank talks.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.162} If you want to say that something has a little more or a little less of a quality than something else, you use \textit{rather, slightly, a bit, a little bit}, or \textit{a little} with comparative adjectives.
\begin{example}
    It's a \underline{rather more complicated} story than that.
    
    She's only \underline{a little bit taller} than her sister.
\end{example}
You also use these forms to say that something has a little more or a little less of a quality than it had before.
\begin{example}
    We must be \underline{rather more visible} to people in the community.
    
    $\ldots$ the little things that made life \underline{slightly less intolerable}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.163} If you want to say emphatically that something has no more of a quality than something else or than it had before, you can use \textit{no} in front of comparative adjectives.
\begin{example}
    Some species of dinosaur were \underline{no bigger} than a chicken.
\end{example}
\textit{Any} is used for emphasis in front of comparatives in negative clauses, questions, and conditional clauses.

E.g., \textit{He wasn't any taller than Jane} means the same as \textit{He was no taller than Jane}.
\begin{example}
    I was 10 and didn't look \underline{any older}.
    
    If it will make you \underline{any happier}, I'll shave off my beard.
    
    Is that \underline{any clearer}?
\end{example}
Note that you only use \textit{no} and \textit{any} like this when comparatives are being used after a linking verb.

You cannot use \textit{no} and \textit{any} with comparatives when they are being used in front of a noun phrase.

E.g., you cannot say \textit{It was a no better meal} or \textit{Is that an any faster train?}

%
\noindent\fbox{2.164} When you use the comparative structure \textit{as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.124--2.130}), submodifying words e.g. \textit{just, quite, nearly}, and \textit{almost} can be used in front of it, modifying the comparison with their usual meanings.
\begin{example}
    Mary was \underline{just as pale as} he was.
    
    There is nothing \underline{quite as lonely as} illness.
    
    $\ldots$ a huge bird which was \underline{nearly as big as} a man.
    
    The land seemed \underline{almost as dark as} the water.
\end{example}
\textit{Nearly} is also used when the \textit{as$\ldots$ as$\ldots$} structure is preceded by \textit{not} with the meaning \textit{less$\ldots$ than}.

You put it after the \textit{not}.

E.g., \textit{I am not nearly as tall as George} means the same as \textit{I am much less tall than George}.
\begin{example}
    This is \underline{not nearly as complicated as} it sounds.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.165} When you use \textit{like} to describe someone or something by comparing them with someone or something else (see paragraphs \textbf{2.132--2.134}), you can use a submodifying adverb in front of it.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ animals that looked \underline{a little like} donkeys.
    
    It's a plane \underline{exactly like} his.
\end{example}
Here is a list of modifying words and phrases used with \textit{like}:
\begin{example}
    a bit, a little, exactly, just, quite, rather, somewhat, very
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.166} When you use \textit{the same as} and \textit{the same} to describe someone or something by saying they are identical to someone or something else, you can use a number of submodifying adverbs in front of them, including \textit{just, exactly, much, nearly, virtually}, and \textit{more or less}.
\begin{example}
    I'm \underline{just the same as} everyone else.
    
    The situation was \underline{much the same} in Germany.
    
    The moral code would seem to be \underline{more or less the same} throughout the world.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.167} When you are using superlative adjectives, you may wish to say that something has much more or much less of a quality than anything else of its kind.

%
The submodifying adverbs \textit{much, quite, easily, by far}, and \textit{very} can be used with the superlative adjectives.

%
\textit{Much, quite}, and \textit{easily} are placed in front of \textit{the} and the superlative.
\begin{example}
    Music may have been \underline{much the most respectable} of his tastes.
    
    $\ldots$ the most frightening time of my life, and \underline{quite the most dishonest}.
    
    This is \underline{easily the best} film of the year.
\end{example}
\textit{By far} can be placed either in front of \textit{the} and the superlative or after the superlative.
\begin{example}
    They are \underline{by far the most dangerous} creatures on the island.
    
    The Union was \underline{the largest by far}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.168} \textit{Very} is only used with superlatives formed by adding \textit{-est} or with irregular superlatives e.g. \textit{1st} or \textit{last}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ in the \underline{very smallest} countries.
    
    $\ldots$ 1 of the \underline{very finest} breeds of dogs.
    
    $\ldots$ on the \underline{very 1st} day of the war.
    
    He had come at the \underline{very last} moment.
    
    That \underline{very next} afternoon he was working in his room.
    
    He spent weeks in that \underline{very same} basement.
\end{example}

\section{Modifying using nouns: noun modifiers}
\noindent\fbox{2.169} Nouns can be used as modifiers in front of other nouns when you want to give more specific information about someone or something.

%
Sometimes, when nouns are used like this they become fixed expressions called \textbf{compound nouns} (see paragraphs \textbf{1.83--1.92}).

%
When the nouns used in front of other nouns are not in fixed expressions, they are called \textbf{noun modifiers}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the \underline{car} door.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{tennis} lessons.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{football} player.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{cat} food.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{music} industry.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{surprise} announcement.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.170} You normally use the singular form of a \textbf{countable noun} (see paragraphs \textbf{1.15--1.22}) as a noun modifier, even when you are referring to more than 1 thing.

E.g., you refer to a shop that sells books as \textit{a book shop}, not \textit{a books shop}, even though it sells a large number of books, not just 1.

%
Many \textbf{plural nouns} lose their \textit{-s} endings when used in front of other nouns.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ my \underline{trouser} pocket.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{pyjama} trousers.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{paratroop} attacks.
\end{example}
Here is a list of common plural nouns that lose their \textit{-s} and \textit{-es} endings when they are used as modifiers:
\begin{example}
    knickers, paratroops, pyjamas, scissors, spectacles, troops, trousers
\end{example}
However, some plural nouns keep the same form when used in front of other nouns.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{arms} control.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{clothes} pegs.
\end{example}
Here is a list of common plural nouns that remain the same when they are used as modifiers:
\begin{example}
    arms, binoculars, clothes, glasses, jeans, sunglasses
\end{example}
Plural nouns are explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.41--1.46}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.171} If you want to be even more specific, you can use more than 1 noun modifier.

E.g., a \textit{car insurance certificate} is a certificate that shows that a car has been insured, and a \textit{state pension scheme} is a scheme that is run by the state and concerns workers' pensions.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{Careers Information} Officer.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{car body repair} kits.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{family dinner} party.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{school medical} officer.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.172} If you want to give more information about a noun that has a noun modifier in front of it, you can put \textbf{adjectives} in front of the noun modifier.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{long} car journey.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{new scarlet} silk handkerchief.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{complex} business deals.
    
    $\ldots$ this \underline{beautiful} morning sunlight.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{French} film industry.
\end{example}
When an adjective comes in front of 2 nouns, it is usually obvious whether it is modifying the 2 nouns combined or only the noun modifier.

%
E.g., in \textit{an electric can opener}, the adjective \textit{electric} is modifying the combination \textit{can opener}, whereas in \textit{electric shock treatment, electric} is modifying the noun \textit{shock} and then both the adjective and the noun modifier are modifying the noun \textit{treatment}.

%
Adjectives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.2--2.102}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.173} \textbf{Proper nouns} can also be used as noun modifiers.

E.g., if you want to show that something is connected with a place, organization, or institution, you put the name of the place, organization, or institution in front of all other noun modifiers.

You also put them in front of classifying adjectives.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{Brighton} Technical College.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{Cambridge House} Literacy Scheme.
\end{example}
Proper nouns are explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.52--1.58}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.174} [$\star$] The use of noun modifiers in English is very common indeed.

In fact, when the context makes it clear what you mean, you can use almost any noun to modify any other noun.

You can use noun modifiers to talk about a wide range of relationships between the 2 nouns.

%
E.g., you can say what something is made of, as in \textit{cotton socks}.

You can also say what is made in a particular place, as in \textit{a glass factory}.

You can say what someone does, as in \textit{a football player}, or you can say where something is, as in \textit{my bedroom curtains}.

%
You can say when something happens, as in \textit{the morning mist} and \textit{he wartime activities}.

You can also describe the nature or size of something, as in \textit{a surprise attack} and \textit{a pocket chess-set}.

\section{Talking about quantities \& amounts}
\noindent\fbox{2.175} This section deals with ways of talking about quantities and amounts of things.

You often refer to quantities by using a number, but sometimes in everyday situations you can do this by using a word or a phrase e.g. \textit{several} or \textit{a lot} and link it with \textit{of} to the following noun.

Quantity expressions like these are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.176--2.193}.

When phrases e.g. \textit{a bottle} are used like this, they are called \textbf{partitives}.

Partitives are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.194--2.207}.

%
When you want to be very precise about the quality or amount of something, you can use \textbf{numbers} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.208--2.239}) or \textbf{fractions} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.240--2.249}).

%
Numbers, fractions, and quantity expressions are also used in expressions of measurement to indicate the size, weight, length, and so on, of something.

Ways of talking about \textbf{measurements} are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.250--2.257}.

Approximate measurements are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.264--2.271}.

Numbers are also used to say how old someone or something is.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.258--2.263}.

\subsection{Talking about amounts of things: \textit{a lot of ideas, plenty of shops}}
\noindent\fbox{2.176} When you want to talk about a quantity of things or an amount of something, you can use the pronoun form of some indefinite determiners (e.g. \textit{all} or \textit{both}) followed by \textit{of} and a noun phrase.
\begin{example}
    I am sure \underline{both of} you agree with me.
    
    \underline{Most of} the population have fled.
    
    \underline{All of} her children live abroad.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.177} Here is the list of indefinite determiners that can be used in this way.

\textit{Of} is given after each as a reminder.
\begin{example}
    all of, another of, any of, both of, each of, either of, enough of, (a) few of, fewer of, less of, (a) little of, lots of, many of, more of, most of, much of, neither of, none of, 1 of, several of, some of
\end{example}
You can also use a phrase e.g. \textit{a lot of} or \textit{a number of} to talk about quantity in the same way.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a house with \underline{lots of} windows.
    
    I make \underline{a lot of} mistakes.
    
    In Tunis there are \underline{a number of} art galleries.
    
    I never found \underline{the rest of} my relatives.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.178} Here is a list of phrases that can be used to talk about quantity.
\begin{example}
    an amount of, a bit of, a little bit of, a couple of, a good deal of, a great deal of, a lot of, a good many of, a great many of, a number of, plenty of, a quantity of
    
    a majority of, the majority of, a minority of
    
    part of, the remainder of, the rest of, the whole of
    
    gobs of (\emph{American}), heaps of, loads of, masses of, tons of
\end{example}
Note that the words in the last group in this list are used in informal speech only.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.179} Some of these quantity expressions are linked by \textit{of} only to noun phrases that begin with a definite determiner e.g. \textit{the, these}, or \textit{my}.

A pronoun e.g. \textit{us, them}, or \textit{these} can also be used after \textit{of}.
\begin{example}
    Nearly \underline{all of the increase} has been caused by inflation.
    
    \underline{Part of the farm} lay close to the river bank.
    
    Only \underline{a few of them} were armed.
\end{example}
Here is a list of quantity expressions with \textit{of} that are usually or always followed by noun phrases beginning with definite determiners:
\begin{example}
    all of, another of, any of, both of, certain of, each of, either of, enough of, few of, fewer of, less of, little of, many of, more of, most of, much of, neither of, none of, 1 of, part of, several of, some of, various of
    
    a few of, a little of, a good many of, a great many of
    
    the remainder of, the rest of, the whole of
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.180} Some of these quantity expressions can also be used with place names.
\begin{example}
    \underline{Much of America} will be shocked by what happened.
    
    $\ldots$ involving \underline{most of Africa} and \underline{a lot of South America}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of quantity expressions used with place names:
\begin{example}
    all of, less of, more of, most of, much of, none of, part of, some of
    
    a bit of, a little bit of, a good deal of, a great deal of, a lot of
    
    the rest of, the whole of
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.181} When you use a quantity expression as the subject of a verb, the verb is singular or plural depending on whether the quantity expression refers to 1 thing or to more than 1 thing.
\begin{example}
    Some of the information \underline{has} already been analyzed.
    
    Some of my best friends \underline{are} policemen.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.182} Many quantity expressions can only be used in front of plural noun phrases.
\begin{example}
    I am sure \underline{both of} you agree with me.
    
    Start by looking through their papers for \underline{either of} the 2 documents mentioned below.
    
    \underline{Few of} these organizations survive for long.
    
    \underline{Several of} his best books are about space flight.
    
    I would like to ask you \underline{a couple of} questions.
    
    The report contained large \underline{numbers of} inaccuracies.
\end{example}
Here is a list of quantity expressions that are only used with plural noun phrases:
\begin{example}
    another of, both of, certain of, each of, either of, few of, fewer of, many of, neither of, numbers of, 1 of, several of, various of
    
    a couple of, a few of, a good many of, a great many of, a number of
\end{example}
For more information about \textit{each of} see paragraphs \textbf{2.186--2.187}, about \textit{fewer of} see paragraph \textbf{2.189}, and about \textit{a number of} see paragraphs \textbf{2.191--2.192}.

%
Note that \textit{neither of} is used in a similar way to \textit{either of} when you are talking about 2 things in negative clauses.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{5.81}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.183} A few quantity expressions are only used with uncountable nouns and singular noun phrases.
\begin{example}
    \underline{Much of} the day was taken up with classes.
    
    This is \underline{a bit of} a change.
    
    There was \underline{a good deal of} smoke.
    
    If you use rich milk, pour off \underline{a little of} the cream.
    
    I spent \underline{the whole of} last year working there.
\end{example}
Here is a list of quantity expressions only used with uncountable nouns and singular noun phrases:
\begin{example}
    less of, little of, much of, part of
    
    a bit of, a little bit of, a good deal of, a great deal of, a little of
    
    the whole of
\end{example}
For more information about \textit{less of} see paragraph \textbf{2.189}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.184} A very few quantity expressions are used only with plural noun phrases and uncountable nouns.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the seizure of vast \underline{quantities} of illegal weapons.
    
    Very large \underline{quantities of} aid were needed.
    
    They had \underline{loads of} things to say about each other.
    
    We had \underline{loads of} room.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{plenty of} the men.
    
    Make sure you give \underline{plenty} of notice.
\end{example}
Here is a list of quantity expressions that are only used with plural noun phrases and uncountable nouns:
\begin{example}
    plenty of, quantities of
    
    gobs of (\emph{American}), heaps of, loads of, masses of, tons of
\end{example}
Note that when the 2nd group in this list are used with an uncountable noun as the subject of a verb, the verb is singular, even though the quantity expression looks plural.
\begin{example}
    Masses and \underline{masses of} food \underline{was} left over.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.185} Some quantity expressions can be used with plural nouns, with singular nouns, or with uncountable nouns.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{some of} the most distinguished men of our time.
    
    We did \underline{some of} the journey by night.
    
    \underline{Some of} the gossip was surprisingly accurate.
\end{example}
Here is a list of quantity expressions that are used with plural nouns, singular nouns, or uncountable nouns:
\begin{example}
    all of, any of, enough of, lots of, more of, most of, none of, some of
    
    an amount of, a lot of, a quantity of
    
    the remainder of, the rest of
\end{example}
Note that \textit{an amount of} is nearly always used with an adjective e.g. \textit{small}: \textit{a small amount of}.

This is explained in paragraph \textbf{2.191}.

%
Note also that when \textit{lots of} is used with an uncountable noun as the subject of a verb, the verb is singular, even though the quantity expression looks plural.
\begin{example}
    He thought that \underline{lots of} lovely money \underline{was} the source of happiness.
\end{example}
\textit{Any of} is explained more fully in paragraphs \textbf{2.188}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.186} [i] When you want to refer to each member of a particular group, you can use \textit{each of} and a plural noun phrase.
\begin{example}
    \underline{Each of} the drawings is slightly different.
    
    We feel quite differently about \underline{each of} our children.
    
    Work out how much you can afford to pay \underline{each of} them.
\end{example}
Note that \textit{each one} and \textit{every one} can be used before \textit{of} instead of \textit{each}, for emphasis.
\begin{example}
    This view of poverty influences \underline{each one} of us.
    
    \underline{Every one} of them is given a financial target.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.187} [!] When the quantity expression \textit{each of} is used with a plural noun phrase, the verb after the noun phrase is always singular.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.188} [i] \textit{Any of} can refer to 1 or several people or things, or to part of something.

Note that if it is the subject of a verb, when it refers to several things, the verb is plural, and when it refers to a part of something, the verb is singular.
\begin{example}
    She has those coats. She might have been wearing \underline{any of} them.
    
    Hardly \underline{any of} these find their way into consumer products.
    
    Has \underline{any of} this been helpful?
    
    It was more expensive than \underline{any of} the other magazines we were normally able to afford.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.189} There are 3 comparative quantity expressions that can be used before noun phrases.

\textit{Less of} is usually used with singular nouns and uncountable nouns, \textit{fewer of} is usually used with plural nouns, and \textit{more of} is used with all 3 types of noun.
\begin{example}
    I enjoy cooking far more now, because I do \underline{less of} it.
    
    \underline{Fewer of} these children will become bored.
    
    He was far \underline{more of} an existentialist.
\end{example}
Note that \textit{more of} is sometimes used in front of a noun phrase to intensify it.
\begin{example}
    He could hardy have felt \underline{more of} a fool than he did at that moment.
    
    She was \underline{more of} a flirt than ever.
    
    America is much \underline{more of} a classless society.
\end{example}
Note also hat \textit{less of} is sometimes used instead of \textit{fewer of}, but many people think that this is not correct.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.190} When the context makes it clear, or when you think that the person you are talking to will understand what you mean, you can sometimes reduce the structure to the quantity word only.

E.g., if you are talking about applications for a job and there were 20 candidates, you can say \textit{Some were very good} rather than \textit{Some of them were very good}.
\begin{example}
    \underline{A few} crossed over the bridge.
    
    Some parts can be separated from \underline{the whole}.
    
    I have 4 bins. I keep one in the kitchen and \underline{the rest} in the dustbin area.
    
    Most of the books had been packed into an enormous trunk and \underline{the remainder} piled on top of it.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.191} [i] You can add adjectives to \textit{a number of} and \textit{a quantity of} to say how large or small an amount or number of things is.
\begin{example}
    The city attracts \underline{a large number of} tourists.
    
    We had \underline{a limited number of} people to choose from.
    
    The novel provides \underline{an enormous quantity of} information.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{a tiny quantity of} acid.
\end{example}
\textit{An amount of} is always used with adjectives, and is usually used with uncountable nouns.
\begin{example}
    Pour \underline{a small amount of} the sauce over the kitchen.
    
    He has \underline{a large amount of} responsibility.
    
    It only involves \underline{a small amount of} time.
    
    There has to be \underline{a certain amount of} hard work.
\end{example}
The plural forms of \textit{quantity, number}, and \textit{amount} are used, especially when referring to separate amounts.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ groups that employ \underline{large numbers of} low-paid workers.
    
    \underline{Enormous amounts of} money are spent on advertising.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.192} When a quantity expression contains an adjective, you can put \textit{very} in front of the adjective.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{a very great deal of} work.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{a very large amount of} money.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.193} Some quantity expressions can be modified using \textit{quite}.
\begin{example}
    I've wasted \underline{quite enough of} my life here.
    
    \underline{Quite a few of} the employees are beginning to realize the truth.
    
    Most of them have had \underline{quite a lot of} experience.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{quite a large amount of} industry.
\end{example}
Here is a list of quantity expressions that can be modified by \textit{quite}:
\begin{example}
    enough, a few, a lot of, a large amount of, a small amount of, a number of, a large number of
\end{example}

\subsection{Talking about particular amounts of things: \textit{a piece of paper, a drop of water}}
\noindent\fbox{2.194} When you want to talk about a particularly quantity of something, you can use a \textbf{partitive} structure that consists of a particular partitive (e.g. \textit{piece}) linked by \textit{of} to another noun.

Partitives are always countable nouns.
\begin{example}
    Who owns this \underline{bit of} land?
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{portions of} mashed potato.
\end{example}
If the partitive is singular, then the verb used with it is usually singular.

If it is plural, the verb is also plural.
\begin{example}
    A piece of paper \underline{is} lifeless.
    
    2 pieces of metal \underline{were} being rubbed together.
\end{example}
Note that all partitives consist of 2 or more words, because \textit{of} is needed in every case.

\textit{Of} is printed in the lists below as a reminder.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.195} When the noun after the partitive is an uncountable noun, you use countable noun e.g. \textit{bit, drop, lump}, or \textit{piece} as the partitive.
\begin{example}
    Here's a \underline{bit of} paper.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{drop of} blood.
    
    \underline{Drops of} sweat dripped from his forehead.
    
    $\ldots$ a tiny \underline{piece of} material.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pinch of} salt.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{specks of} dust.
\end{example}
These partitives can be used without \textit{of} when it is obvious what you are talking about.
\begin{example}
    He sat down in the kitchen before a plate of cold ham, but he had only eaten 1 \underline{piece} when the phone rang.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.196} Here is a list of partitives used with uncountable nouns:
\begin{example}
    amount of, bit of, blob of, chunk of, dash of, drop of, grain of, heap of, knob of, lump of, mass of, morsel of, mountain of, piece of, pile of, pinch of, pool of, portion of, scrap of, sheet of, shred of, slice of, speck of, spot of, touch of, trace of
\end{example}
Some of these partitives are also used with plural nouns referring to things that together form a mass.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a huge \underline{heap of} stones.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pile of} newspapers.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives used with both uncountable and plural nouns:
\begin{example}
    amount of, clump of, heap of, mass of, mountain of, pile of, portion of
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.197} [$\star$] Many nouns that refer to the shape of an amount of something can also be partitives with uncountable or plural nouns.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{ball of} wool.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{columns of} smoke.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{ring of} excited faces.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives used for talking about the shape of an amount of something:
\begin{example}
    ball of, column of, ring of, shaft of, square of, stick of, strip of, thread of, tuft of, wall of
\end{example}
Many nouns that refer to both shape and movement can also be used as partitives.
\begin{example}
    It blew a \underline{jet of} water into the air.
    
    $\ldots$ a constant \underline{stream of} children passing through the door.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives used for talking about both shape and movement:
\begin{example}
    dribble of, gush of, gust of, jet of, shower of, spurt of, stream of, torrent of
\end{example}
%
[$\star$] You can use any noun to describe shape in this way.

E.g., you can talk about \textit{a triangle of snooker balls}.

\noindent\fbox{2.198} There are many nouns that refer to groups that can be used as partitives.

They are linked by \textit{of} to plural nouns that indicate what the group consists of.
\begin{example}
    It was evaluated by an independent \underline{team of} inspectors.
    
    A \underline{group of} journalists gathered at the airport to watch us take off.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{bunch of} flowers.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives referring to groups:
\begin{example}
    audience of, bunch of, clump of, company of, family of, flock of, gang of, group of, herd of, team of, troupe of
\end{example}
[$\star$] You can use any noun referring to a group of people or things in this way.

E.g., you can talk about \textit{an army of volunteers}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.199} Nouns referring to measurements are often used in partitive structures to talk about an amount of something that is a particular length, area, volume, or weight.

Uncountable nouns are used after \textit{of} in structures referring to length, and both uncountable and plural nouns are used in structures referring to weight.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ 10 \underline{yards of} velvet.
    
    Thousands of \underline{square miles of} land have been contaminated.
    
    I drink a \underline{pint of} milk a day.
    
    $\ldots$ 3 \underline{pounds of} strawberries.
    
    $\ldots$ 10 \underline{ounces of} cheese.
\end{example}
Nouns referring to measurements are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.250--2.257}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.200} You use partitives when you want to talk about the contents of a container as well as to the container itself.

E.g., you can refer to a carton filled with milk as \textit{a carton of milk}.
\begin{example}
    I went to buy a \underline{bag of} chips.
    
    The waiter appeared with a \underline{bottle of} red wine.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{packet of} cigarettes.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pot of} honey.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{tubes of} glue.
\end{example}
You can also use partitives to refer to the contents only.
\begin{example}
    They drank another \underline{bottle of} champagne.
    
    She ate a whole \underline{box of} chocolates.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives referring to containers:
\begin{example}
    bag of, barrel of, basin of, basket of, bottle of, bowl of, box of, bucket of, can of, carton of, case of, cask of, crate of, cup of, glass of, jar of, jug of, mug of, pack of, packet of, plate of, pot of, sack of, spoon of, tablespoon of, tank of, teaspoon of, tin of, tub of, tube of, tumbler of
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.201} You can add \textit{-ful} to these partitives referring to containers.
\begin{example}
    He brought me a \underline{bagful of} sweets.
    
    Pour a \underline{bucketful of} cold water on the ash.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{cupful of} boiled water.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{tankful of} petrol.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives referring to containers that are very commonly used with \textit{-ful}:
\begin{example}
    bag, basket, box, bucket, cup, plate, spoon, tablespoon, tank, teaspoon
\end{example}
When people want to make a noun ending in \textit{-ful} plural, they usually add an \textit{-s} to the end of the word, as in \textit{bucketfuls}.

However some people put the \textit{-s} in front of \textit{-ful}, as in \textit{bucketsful}.
\begin{example}
    She ladled 3 \underline{spoonfuls of} sugar into my tea.
    
    They were collecting \underline{basketfuls of} apples.
    
    $\ldots$ 2 \underline{teaspoonfuls of} powder.
    
    $\ldots$ 2 \underline{teaspoonsful} of milk.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.202} [$\star$] You can also add \textit{-ful} to other partitives.
\begin{example}
    Eleanor was holding an \underline{armful of} red roses.
    
    I went outside to throw a \underline{handful of} bread to the birds.
    
    He took another \underline{mouthful of} whisky.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{houseful of} children.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.203} [i] You sometimes use a mass noun instead of a partitive structure.

E.g., \textit{2 teas} means the same as \textit{2 cups of tea}, and \textit{2 sugars} means \textit{2 spoonfuls of sugar}.
\begin{example}
    We drank a couple of \underline{beers}.
    
    I asked for 2 \underline{coffees} with milk.
\end{example}
\textbf{Mass nouns} are explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.28--1.31}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.204} You use a partitive when you want to talk about a part or a fraction of a particular thing.
\begin{example}
    I spent a large \underline{part of} my life in broadcasting.
    
    The system is breaking down in many \underline{parts of} Africa.
    
    A large \underline{portion of} the university budget goes into the Community Services area.
    
    $\ldots$ a mass movement involving all \underline{segments of} society.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives referring to a part of something:
\begin{example}
    part of, portion of, section of, segment of
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.205} You use a partitive with an uncountable noun referring to things of a certain type when you want to refer to 1 particular thing of that type.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ an \underline{article} of clothing.
    
    I bought a few \underline{bits of} furniture.
    
    Any \underline{item of} information can be accessed.
\end{example}
Here is a list of partitives referring to 1 thing of a particular type:
\begin{example}
    article of, bit of, item of, piece of
\end{example}
Here is a list of uncountable nouns referring to things of a certain type that are often used with the partitives listed above:
\begin{example}
    advice, apparatus, baggage, clothing, equipment, furniture, homework, information, knowledge, luggage, machinery, news, research
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.206} Some plural nouns refer to things that are normally thought to consist of 2 parts, e.g. trousers or scissors.

Some others refer to things that are made in twos, e.g. shoes or shocks.

When you want to talk about 1 of these 2-part items, or 2-item sets, you use the partitive \textit{pair} linked to these plural nouns by \textit{of}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pair of} jeans.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{pair of} tights.
    
    $\ldots$ a dozen \underline{pairs of} sunglasses.
    
    I brought a \underline{pair of} tennis shoes.
    
    I smashed 3 \underline{pairs of} skis.
\end{example}
These \textbf{plural nouns} are explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.41--1.46}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.207} [$\star$] Whenever you want to
\begin{itemize}
    \item[(i)] talk about a limited amount of something,
    \item[(ii)] indicate the area that something occupies, or
    \item[(iii)] specify a particular feature that something has,
\end{itemize}
you can use a noun that indicates the amount or the nature of the thing (e.g. \textit{bottle}), linked by \textit{of} to a noun that indicates what the thing is (e.g. \textit{water}).

%
E.g., if you say \textit{a forest of pines}, you are talking about a large area of trees.

Similarly, you can talk about \textit{a border of roses}.

%
This structure can be extended very widely, so that you can talk about \textit{a city of dreaming spires}, e.g.

\section{Referring to an exact number of things: numbers}
\noindent\fbox{2.208} When you want to refer to an exact number of things, you use numbers e.g. \textit{two, thirty}, and \textit{777}, which are called \textbf{cardinal numbers}, or sometimes \textbf{cardinals}.
\begin{example}
    I'm going to ask you \underline{30} questions.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{260} copies of the record.
\end{example}
The cardinal numbers are listed in the Reference section and their use is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.213--2.231}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.209} When you want to identify or describe something by showing where it comes in a series or sequence, you use an \textbf{ordinal number}, or an \textbf{ordinal}, e.g. \textit{1st, 2nd, 14th}, or \textit{27th}.
\begin{example}
    She received a video camera for her \underline{14th} birthday.
    
    I repeated my story for the \underline{3rd} time that day.
\end{example}
The ordinal numbers are listed in the Reference section and their use is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.232--2.239}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.210} When you want to show how large a part of something is compared to the whole of it, you use a \textbf{fraction} e.g. \textit{a 3rd} or \textit{3-quarters}.
\begin{example}
    \underline{A 3rd} of the American forces were involved.
    
    The bottle was about \underline{3-quarters} full when he started.
\end{example}
Fractions are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.240--2.249}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.211} When you want to talk about a size, distance, area, volume, weight, speed, or temperature, you can do so by using a number or quantity expression in front of a \textbf{measurement noun} e.g. \textit{feet} and \textit{miles}.
\begin{example}
    He was about 6 \underline{feet} tall.
    
    It's 4 \underline{miles} to the city center from here.
\end{example}
Measurements nouns are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.250--2.257}.

%
If you do not know the exact number, size, or quantity of something, you can give an approximate amount of measurement using 1 of a group of special words and expressions.

These are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.263--2.271}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.212} When you want to say how old someone or something is, you have a choice of ways in which to do it.

These are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.258--2.263}.

\subsection{Talking about the number of things: cardinal numbers}
\noindent\fbox{2.213} If you want to talk about some or all of the things in a group, you can show how many things you are talking about by using a \textbf{cardinal number}.

%
The cardinal numbers are listed in the Reference section.
\begin{example}
    By Christmas, we had \underline{10} cows.
\end{example}
When you use a determiner and a number in front of a noun, you put the determiner in front of the number.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{the 3} young men.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{my 2} daughters.
    
    Watch the eyes of \underline{any 2} people engrossed in conversation.
    
    \underline{All 3} candidates are coming to Blackpool later this week.
\end{example}
When you put a number and an adjective in front of a noun, you usually put the number in front of the adjective.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{2 small} children.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{1500 local} residents.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{3 beautiful young} girls.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.214} \textit{One} is used as a number in front of a noun to emphasize that there is only 1 thing, to show that you are being precise, or to contrast 1 thing with another.

\textit{One} is followed by a singular noun.
\begin{example}
    That is the \underline{1} big reservation I've got.
    
    He balanced himself on \underline{1} foot.
    
    There was only \underline{1} gate into the palace.
    
    This treaty was signed \underline{1} year after the Suez Crisis.
    
    It was negative in \underline{1} respect but positive in another. 
\end{example}
\textit{One} is also used, like other numbers, as a quantity expression.
\begin{example}
    \underline{1 of} my students sold me her ticket.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{1 of} the few great novels of the century.
    
    It's \underline{1 of} the best films I've ever seen.
\end{example}
\textit{One} also has special uses a determiner and a pronoun.

These are explained in paragraph \textbf{1.249} and paragraphs \textbf{1.158--1.161}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.215} When a large number begins with the figure \textit{1}, the \textit{1} can be said or written as \textit{a} or \textit{one}.

\textit{One} is more formal.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{a million} dollars.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{a hundred and fifty} miles.
    
    Over \underline{one million} pounds has been raised.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.216} The number 0 is not used in ordinary English to say that the number of things you are talking about is zero.

Instead the negative determiner \textit{no} or the negative pronoun \textit{none} is used, or \textit{any} is used with a negative.

These are explained in paragraphs \textbf{5.51} and \textbf{5.71--5.73}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.217} When you use any number except \textit{one} in front of a noun, you use a plural noun.
\begin{example}
    There were 10 \underline{people} there, all men.
    
    $\ldots$ 100 \underline{years}.
    
    $\ldots$ 101 \underline{things}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.218} When you use a number and a plural noun to talk about 2 or more things, you usually use a plural verb.

You use a singular verb with \textit{one}.
\begin{example}
    7 guerrillas \underline{were} wounded.
    
    There is \underline{1} clue.
\end{example}
However, when you are talking about an amount of money or time, or a distance, speed, or weight, you usually use a number, a plural noun, and a singular verb.
\begin{example}
    300 pounds \underline{is} a lot of money.
    
    10 years \underline{is} a long time.
    
    26 miles \underline{is} a long way to run.
    
    90 miles an hour \underline{is} much too fast.
    
    90 pounds \underline{is} all she weighs.
\end{example}
Ways of measuring things are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.250--2.257}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.219} You can use cardinal numbers with both \textbf{ordinals} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.232--2.239}) and \textbf{postdeterminers} (see paragraph \textbf{2.40}).

When you use a cardinal number with a determiner followed by an ordinal number of a postdeterminer, the cardinal number usually comes after the determiner and the ordinal or postdeterminer.
\begin{example}
    The \underline{1st 2} years have been very successful.
    
    $\ldots$ throughout the \underline{1st 4} months of this year.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{last 2} volumes of the encyclopedia.
    
    $\ldots$ in the \underline{previous 3} years of his reign.
\end{example}
Note that some postdeterminers can be used like ordinary \textbf{classifying adjectives} (see paragraph \textbf{2.40}).

When they are used like this, the cardinal number comes before them.
\begin{example}
    He has written \underline{2 previous} novels.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{2 further} examples.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.220} When either the context makes it clear, or you think that your listener already knows something, you can use the cardinal number without a noun.
\begin{example}
    These \underline{2} are quite different.
\end{example}
When cardinal numbers are used like this, you can put ordinal numbers, postdeterminers, or superlatives adjectives in between the determiner and the cardinal number.
\begin{example}
    I want to tell you about the programmes. \underline{The 1st 4} are devoted to universities.
    
    \underline{The other 6} are masterpieces.
    
    \underline{The best 30} have the potential to be successful journalists.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.221} When you use \textit{dozen, hundred, thousand, million}, or \textit{billion} to indicate exact numbers, you put \textit{a} or another number in front of them.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{a hundred} dollars.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{610} miles.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{a thousand billion} pounds.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{2 dozen} diapers.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.222} [!] When you use \textit{dozen, hundred, thousand, million}, or \textit{billion} they remain singular even when the number in front of them is greater than 1.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.223} [!] You can use \textit{dozen, hundred, thousand, million}, or \textit{billion} without \textit{of} in a less precise way by putting \textit{several, a few}, and \textit{a couple of} in front of them.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{several hundred} people.
    
    \underline{A few thousand} cars have gone.
    
    $\ldots$ life \underline{a couple of hundred} years ago.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.224} When you want to emphasize how large a number is without stating it precisely, you can use \textit{dozens, hundreds, thousands, millions}, and \textit{billions} in the same way as cardinals followed by \textit{of}.
\begin{example}
    That's going to take \underline{hundreds of} years.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{hundreds of} dollars.
    
    We traveled \underline{thousands of} miles across Europe.
    
    $\ldots$ languages spoken by \underline{millions of} people.
    
    We have \underline{dozens of} friends in the community.
\end{example}
You can put \textit{many} in front of these plural forms.
\begin{example}
    I have traveled \underline{many hundreds of} miles with them.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.225} [i] People often use the plural forms when they are exaggerating.
\begin{example}
    I was meeting \underline{thousands of} people.
    
    Do you have to fill in \underline{hundreds of} forms before you go?
\end{example}
You can also emphasize or exaggerate a large number of using these words in phrases beginning with \textit{by}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a book which sells \underline{by the million}.
    
    $\ldots$ people who give injections \underline{by the dozen}.
    
    Videos of the royal wedding \underline{sold by the hundred thousand}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.226} Cardinal numbers are used to label or identify things.
\begin{example}
    Room \underline{777} of the Stanley Hotel.
    
    Number \underline{11} Downing Street.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.227} You can also use cardinal numbers as quantity expressions linked by \textit{of} to a noun phrase referring to a group.

You do this when you want to emphasize that you are talking about a part or all of a group.
\begin{example}
    I saw \underline{4 of} these programmes.
    
    \underline{3 of} the questions today have been about democracy.
    
    I use plastic kitchen bins. I have \underline{4 of} them.
    
    \underline{All 8 of} my great-grandparents lived in the city.
    
    \underline{All 4 of} us wanted to get away from the Earl's Court area.
    
    The clerk looked at \underline{the 6 of} them and said, All of you?
    
    I find it less worrying than \underline{the 2 of} you are suggesting.
\end{example}
Quantity expressions are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.176--2.193}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.228} Cardinal numbers are used to quantify something without the \textit{of} and the noun phrase, when it is clear what you are referring to.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a group of painters, \underline{9} or \underline{10} in all.
    
    Of the other wives, \underline{2} are dancers and \underline{1} is a singer.
    
    $\ldots$ the taller student of \underline{the 2}.
    
    $\ldots$ breakfast for \underline{2}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.229} Cardinal numbers are also used after subject pronouns.
\begin{example}
    In the fall \underline{we 2} are going to England.
    
    \underline{You 4}, come with me.
\end{example}
%
