\noindent\fbox{2.230} Cardinal numbers can be used as part of a \textbf{compound adjective} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.94--2.102}).

The cardinal number is used in front of a noun to form a compound adjective that is usually hyphenated
\begin{example}
    He took out a \underline{5-dollar} bill.
    
    I wrote a \underline{5-page} summary of the situation.
\end{example}
Note that the noun remains singular even when the number is 2 or more, and that compound adjectives that are formed like this cannot be used after a linking verb.

E.g., you cannot say \textit{My essay is five-hundred-word}.

Instead you would probably say \textit{My essay is 500 words long}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.231} Cardinal numbers are sometimes used with general time words e.g. \textit{month} and \textit{week}.

You do this when you want to describe something by saying how long it lasts.

If the thing is referred to with an uncountable noun, you use the \textbf{possessive form} (see paragraphs \textbf{1.211--1.222}) of the general time word.
\begin{example}
    She's already had at least \underline{9 months'} experience.
    
    On Friday she had been given \underline{2 weeks'} notice.
\end{example}
Sometimes the apostrophe is omitted.
\begin{example}
    They wanted \underline{3 weeks} holiday and \underline{3 weeks} pay.
\end{example}
The determiner \textit{a} is usually used when you are talking about a single period of time, although \textit{one} can be used instead when you want to be more formal.
\begin{example}
    She's on \underline{a year's} leave from Hunter College.
    
    He was only given \underline{1 week's} notice.
\end{example}
Cardinal numbers are also used with general time words as modifiers of adjectives.
\begin{example}
    She was \underline{4 months} pregnant.
    
    The rains are \underline{2 months} late.
    
    His rent was \underline{3 weeks} overdue.
\end{example}

\subsection{Referring to things in a sequence: ordinal numbers}
\noindent\fbox{2.232} If you want to identify or describe something by saying where it comes in a series or sequence, you use an \textbf{ordinal number}.
\begin{example}
    Quietly they took their seats in the \underline{1st} 3 rows.
    
    Flora's flat is on the \underline{4th} floor of this 5-storey block.
    
    They stopped at the \underline{1st} of the trees.
\end{example}
Note that you can also use \textit{following, last, next, preceding, previous}, and \textit{subsequent} like ordinal numbers to say where something comes in a series or sequence.
\begin{example}
    The \underline{following} morning he checked out of the hotel.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{last} rungs of the fire-escape.
    
    $\ldots$ at the \underline{next} general election.
    
    The \underline{preceding} text has been professionally transcribed.
    
    I mentioned this in a \underline{previous} programme.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{subsequent} career patterns of those taking degrees.
\end{example}
\textit{Following, subsequent, previous}, and \textit{preceding} are only used to indicate the position of something in a sequence in time or in a piece of writing.

\textit{Next} and \textit{last} are used more generally, e.g. to refer to things in rows or lists.

%
The ordinal numbers are listed in the Reference section.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.233} Ordinals are often used in front of nouns.

They are not usually used after linking verbs like \textit{be}.

They are usually preceded by a determiner.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{the 1st} day of autumn.
    
    He took the lift to \underline{the 16th} floor.
    
    $\ldots$ on \underline{her 21st} birthday.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{his father's 2nd} marriage.
\end{example}
In some idiomatic phrases ordinals are used without determiners.
\begin{example}
    The picture seems \underline{at 1st glance} chaotic.
    
    I might. \underline{On 2nd thoughts}, no.
    
    \underline{1st children} usually get a lot of attention.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.234} Ordinals can be written in abbreviated form, e.g. in dates or headings or in very informal writing.

You write the last 2 letters of the ordinal after the number expressed in figures.

E.g., \textit{first} can be written as \textit{1st, twenty-second} as \textit{22nd, hundred and thirds} as \textit{103rd}, and \textit{fourteenth} as \textit{14th}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ on August \underline{2nd}.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{1st} Division of the Sovereign's Escort.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{11th} Cavalry.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.235} You can specify which group the thing referred to by an ordinal belongs to by using the preposition \textit{of} after the ordinal.
\begin{example}
    It is \underline{the 3rd of} a series of 8 programmes.
    
    Tony was \underline{the 2nd of} 4 sons.
\end{example}
When ordinals are used like this, they usually refer to 1 person or thing.

However, when they are used with a \textit{to}-infinitive, or another phrase or clause after them, they can refer to 1 person or thing or to more than 1.

\textit{1st} is used like this more than the other ordinals.
\begin{example}
    I was \underline{the 1st to recover}.
    
    They had to be \underline{the 1st to go}.
    
    The proposals - \underline{the 1st for 22 years} - amount to a new charter for the mentally ill.
    
    The withdrawals were \underline{the 1st that the army agreed to}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.236} You can use an ordinal to refer to a member of a group that you have already mentioned or to something of the kind already mentioned, and you can omit the noun that identifies the thing.
\begin{example}
    In Aug 1932 2 of the group's members were expelled from the party and \underline{a 3rd} was suspended.
    
    The 3rd child tries to outdo \underline{the 1st and 2nd}.
    
    A 2nd pheasant flew up. Then \underline{a third} and \underline{a 4th}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.237} The adjectives \textit{next} and \textit{last} can be used, like ordinals, by themselves when the context makes the meaning clear.
\begin{example}
    You missed 1 meal. \underline{The meal} is on the table in half an hour.
    
    Smithy removed \underline{the last} of the crews.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.238} The ordinal \textit{1st} is also used as an adverb to show that something is done before other things.

Other ordinals are also sometimes used to show the order in which things are done, especially in informal English.

People also use ordinals as adverbs when they are giving a list of points, reasons, or items.

This is explained fully in paragraph \textbf{10.54}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.239} The use of ordinals in expressing fractions is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.241--2.243}.

The use of ordinals to express dates, as in \textit{the 17th of June}, is explained in paragraph \textbf{4.88}.

%
Ordinal numbers can be used in front of cardinal numbers.

This is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.219--2.220}.

\subsection{Talking about an exact part of something: fractions}
\noindent\fbox{2.240} When you want to show how small or large a part of something is compared to the whole of it, you use a \textbf{fraction}, e.g. \textit{a 3rd}, followed by \textit{of} and a noun referring to the whole thing.

Fractions can also be written in figures (see paragraph \textbf{2.248}).

%
\noindent\fbox{2.241} When you express a fraction in words, the way you do so depends on whether the fraction is singular or plural.

If it is singular, you write or say an \textbf{ordinal number} or the special fraction terms \textit{half} or \textit{quarter}, with either the number \textit{one} or a determiner e.g. \textit{a} in front of them.

The fraction is linked to a noun by \textit{of}.
\begin{example}
    This state produces \underline{a 3rd} of the nation's oil.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{a quarter} of an inch.
    
    You can take \underline{a 5th} of your money out on demand.
    
    \underline{A 10th of} our budget goes on fuel.
    
    Forests cover \underline{1 3rd} of the country.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{100th} of a degree.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{1 quarter} of the total population.
\end{example}
An adjective can also be placed after the determiner and before the fraction.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{the 1st half} of the 20th century.
    
    I read \underline{the 1st half} of the book.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{the southern half} of England.
    
    $\ldots$ in \underline{the 1st quarter} of 2004.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.242} [i] If you are using \textit{half} in front of a pronoun, you still use \textit{of} after the \textit{half}.
\begin{example}
    Nearly \underline{half of it} comes from the Middle East.
    
    More than \underline{half of them} have gone home.
    
    \underline{Half of us} have lost our jobs.
\end{example}
Note that when the fraction \textit{a half} is used with \textit{of}, you usually write or say it as \textit{half} without a determiner.

\textit{A half} and \textit{one half} are rarely used.
\begin{example}
    They lost \underline{half of} their pay.
    
    \underline{Half of} the people went to private schools.
    
    I had crossed more than \underline{half of} America.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.243} If the fraction is plural, you put a \textbf{cardinal number} in front of a plural form of the \textbf{ordinal number} or special fraction word \textit{quarter}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the poorer \underline{2 3rds} of the world.
    
    The journey is going to take \underline{3 quarters} of an hour.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{4 5ths} of the money.
    
    \underline{9 10th} of them live on the land.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{3 millionths} of a centimeter.
\end{example}
When \textit{half} is used with whole numbers or amounts, it is used with the determiner \textit{a}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{1 and a half acres} of land.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{4 and a half} centuries.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.244} When you talk about fractions of a single thing, you use a singular form of a verb afterwards.
\begin{example}
    Half of our work \underline{is} to design programmes.
    
    2 3rds of the planet's surface \underline{is} covered with water.
    
    2 5ths of the forest \underline{was} removed.
\end{example}
However, when you talk about fractions of a number of things, you use a plural form of a verb afterwards.
\begin{example}
    2 3rds of Chad's exports \underline{were} cotton.
    
    A quarter of the students \underline{were} seen individually.
    
    More than half of these photographs \underline{are} of her.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.245} When it is clear to your listener or reader who or what you are referring to, either because of the context or because you and your listener or reader know what is meant, you can use fraction as pronouns without the \textit{of} and noun after them.
\begin{example}
    Of the people who work here, \underline{half} are French and \underline{half} are English.
    
    \underline{2 3rds} were sterilized.
    
    \underline{1 6th} are disappointed with the service.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.246} Besides their use as quantity expressions linked by \textit{of} to a noun phrase, fractions can also be used after a whole number or amount plus \textit{and}, with a noun placed after the fraction.

The noun must be plural even if the number is \textit{1}.
\begin{example}
    You've got to sit there for \underline{1 and a half hours}.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{5 and a quarter days}.
    
    $\ldots$ more than \underline{4 and a half} centuries ago.
\end{example}
If you are using \textit{a} instead of \textit{one}, the \textit{and} and the fraction come after the noun.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{a mile and a half} below the surface.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{a mile and a quarter} of motorway.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.247} Besides being used with \textit{of} as a quantity expression, \textit{half} is also used as a \textbf{predeterminer} (see paragraph \textbf{1.251}), directly in front of a determiner.
\begin{example}
    I met \underline{half} the girls at the conference.
    
    The farmers sold off \underline{half} their land.
    
   $\ldots$ \underline{half} a pound of coffee.
   
   $\ldots$ \underline{half} a bottle of milk.
\end{example}
Note that \textit{half} is always used with \textit{of} before a pronoun (see paragraph \textbf{2.242}).

%
\noindent\fbox{2.248} You can write a fraction in figures, e.g. $\frac{1}{2},\frac{1}{4},\frac{3}{4}$, and $\frac{2}{3}$.

These correspond to \textit{a half, a quarter, 3 quarters} and \textit{2 3rds}, resp.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.249} Fractions are often given in a special form as a number of 100ths.

This type of fraction is called a \textbf{percentage}.

E.g., \textit{3 100ths}, expressed as a percentage, is \textit{3 per cent}.

It can also be written as \textit{3 percent} or \textit{3\%}.

\textit{A half} can be expressed as \textit{50 per cent, 50 percent}, or \textit{50\%}.
\begin{example}
    \underline{90 percent} of most food is water.
    
    About \underline{20 per cent} of student accountants are women.
    
    Before 1960 \underline{45\%} of British trade was with the Commonwealth.
\end{example}
You use percentages on their own as noun phrases when it is clear what they refer to.
\begin{example}
    \underline{90 per cent} were self employed.
    
    $\ldots$ interest at \underline{10\%} per annum.
\end{example}

\subsection{Talking about measurements}
\noindent\fbox{2.250} You can refer to a size, distance, area, volume, weight, speed, or temperature by using a number of quantity expression in front of a \textbf{measurement noun}.

Measurement nouns are countable.
\begin{example}
    They grow to 20 \underline{feet}.
    
    $\ldots$ blocks of stone weighting up to a hundred \underline{tons}.
    
    Reduce the temperature by a few \underline{degrees}.
    
    Average annual temperatures exceed \underline{$20^\circ$ centigrade}.
\end{example}
Other ways of expressing distance are explained in paragraphs \textbf{6.91--6.92}.

Measurement nouns referring to size, area, volume, and weight are often used in \textbf{partitive structures} (see paragraphs \textbf{2.194--2.207}) e.g. \textit{a pint of milk} and \textit{a pound of onions}.

They are also used in phrases beginning with \textit{of} (see paragraph \textbf{2.283}).

%
\noindent\fbox{2.251} There are 2 systems of measurement used in Britain - the \textbf{imperial system} and the \textbf{metric system}.

Each system has its own measurement nouns.

%
Here is a list of the imperial units of measurement indicating size, distance, area, volume, and weight:
\begin{example}
    inch, foot, yard, mile
    
    acre
    
    pint, quart, gallon
    
    ounce, pound, stone, hundredweight, ton
\end{example}
Note that the plural of \textit{foot} is \textit{feet}, but \textit{foot} can also be used with numbers.

Similarly \textit{stone} is usually used instead of \textit{stones}.

%
Here is a list of the metric units of measurement indicating size, distance, area, volume, and weight:
\begin{example}
    millimeter, centimeter, meter, kilometer
    
    hectare
    
    milliliter, centiliter, liter
    
    milligram, gram, kilogram, tonne
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.252} Measurement nouns are often used after \textbf{linking verbs} e.g. \textit{be, measure} and \textit{weigh}.
\begin{example}
    The fish \underline{was} about 8 feet long.
    
    It \underline{measures} approximately 26 inches wide $\times 25$ inches long.
    
    $\ldots$ a square area \underline{measuring} 900 meters on each side.
    
    It \underline{weighs} 50 or more kilos.
\end{example} 
The use of adjectives after linking verbs is explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.132--3.137}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.253} When measurement nouns that give the size of something are used after a linking verb, they are often followed by an adjective that makes it clear exactly what the measurement refers to
\begin{example}
    He was about \underline{6 feet tall}.
    
    The spears were about \underline{6 foot long}.
    
    $\ldots$ a room \underline{2 meters wide}.
    
    The water was \underline{15 feet deep}.
    
    $\ldots$ a layer of stone \underline{4 meters thick}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of the adjectives that follow measurement nouns indicating size:
\begin{example}
    broad, deep, high, long, tall, thick, wide
\end{example}
Note that you do not say \textit{2 pounds heavy} but \textit{2 pounds in weight} instead.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.254} Similarly, some measurement nouns can be followed by prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{in}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a block of ice 1 cubic foot \underline{in size}.
    
    I put on nearly a stone \underline{in weight}.
    
    They are 30 centimeters \underline{in length}.
    
    $\ldots$ deposits measuring up to a kilometer \underline{in thickness}.
    
    It was close to 10 feet \underline{in height}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of phrases beginning with \textit{in} used after measurements:
\begin{example}
    in area, in breadth, in depth, in distance, in height, in length, in size, in thickness, in volume, in weight, in width
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.255} Measurement nouns can also be used as modifiers in front of a noun when you want to describe things in terms of their measurements.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a \underline{5 foot 9 inch} bed.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{70 foot high} mounds of dust.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{$12\times 12$ inch} tiles.
    
    $\ldots$ a \underline{5-pound} bag of lentils.
\end{example}
Note that the measurement noun is singular.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.256} [i] If you want to describe fully the size of an object or area, you can give its dimensions; i.e., you give measurements of its length and width, or length, width, and depth.

When you give the dimensions of an object, you separate the figures using \textit{and, by}, or the multiplication sign $\times$.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ planks of wood about \underline{3 inches thick and 2 feet wide}.
    
    The island measures about \underline{25 miles by 12 miles}.
    
    Lake Nyasa is \underline{450 miles long by about 50 miles wide}.
    
    The box measures approximately \underline{26 inches wide $\times$ 25 inches deep $\times$ 16 inches high}.
\end{example}
If you are talking about a square object or area, you give the length of each side followed by the word \textit{square}.
\begin{example}
    Each family hs only 1 room 8 or 10 feet \underline{square}.
    
    The site measures roughly 35 feet \underline{square}.
\end{example}
\textit{Square} is used in front of units of length when expressing data.

\textit{Cubic} is used in front of units of length when expressing volume.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a farm covering 300 \underline{square miles}.
    
    The brain of the 1st ape-men was about the same size as that of gorilla, around 500 \underline{cubic centimeters}.
\end{example}
You express temperature in degrees, using either degrees centigrade, or degrees Fahrenheit.

Note that in everyday language the metric term \textit{centigrade} is used to indicate temperature, whereas in scientific language the term \textit{Celsius} is used which refers to exactly the same scale of measurement.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.257} You talk about the speed of something by saying how far it can travel in a particular unit of time.

To do this, you use a noun e.g. \textit{mile} or \textit{kilometer}, followed by \textit{per, a}, or \textit{an}, and a time noun.
\begin{example}
    The car would do only 45 \underline{miles per hour}.
    
    Wind speeds at the airport were 160 \underline{kilometers per hour}.
    
    Warships move at about 500 \underline{miles per day}.
\end{example}

\subsection{Talking about age}
\noindent\fbox{2.258} When you want to say how old someone is, you have a choice of ways in which to do it.

You can be exact or approximate.

Similarly, when you ant to say how old something is, you can use different ways, some exact, and some approximate.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.259} When you want to talk about a person's exact age, you can do so by using
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textit{be} followed by a number, and sometimes \textit{years old} after the number.
    \begin{example}
        I \underline{was 19}, and he \underline{was 21}.
        
        I'\underline{m} only \underline{63}.
        
        She \underline{is 25 years old}.
        
        I \underline{am 40 years old}.
    \end{example}
    \item \textit{of} (or less commonly \textit{aged}) and a number after a noun
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a child \underline{of 6}.
        
        $\ldots$ 2 little boys \underline{aged} about \underline{9 and 11}.
    \end{example}
    \item a compound adjective, usually hyphenated, consisting of a number, followed by a singular noun referring to a period of time, followed by \textit{old}
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a \underline{twenty-two-year-old} student.
        
        $\ldots$ a \underline{5-month-old} baby.
        
        $\ldots$ a pretty \underline{350-year-old} cottage.
        
        $\ldots$ a violation of a \underline{6-year-old} agreement.
    \end{example}
    \item a compound noun consisting of a number followed by \textit{-year-old}
    \begin{example}
        The servant was a pale little \underline{14-year-old} who looked hardly more than 10.
        
        All the \underline{6-year-olds} are taught by 1 teacher.
        
        $\ldots$ Melvin Kalkhoven, a tall, thin \underline{35-year-old}.
    \end{example}
\end{itemize}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.260} When you want to talk about a person's age in an approximate way, you can do so by using
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textit{in}, followed by a possessive determiner, followed by a plural noun referring to a particular range of years e.g. \textit{twenties} and \textit{teens}
    \begin{example}
        He was \underline{in his sixties}.
        
        I didn't mature till I was in \underline{my forties}.
        
        $\ldots$ the groups who are now \underline{in their thirties}.
        
        $\ldots$ when I was \underline{in my teens}.
    \end{example}
    Note that you can use \textit{early, mid-, middle}, or \textit{late} to indicate approximately where someone's age comes in a particular range of years.
    \begin{example}
        He was then \underline{in his late seventies}.
        
        She was \underline{in her mid-twenties}.
        
        Jame is only \underline{in her early forties}.
    \end{example}
    \item \textit{over} or \textit{under} followed by a number
    \begin{example}
        She was well \underline{over 50}.
        
        She was only a little \underline{over 40 years old}.
        
        There weren't enough people who were \underline{under 25}.
    \end{example}
    Note that you can also use \textit{above} or \textit{below} followed by \textit{the age of} and a number.
    \begin{example}
        55 per cent of them were \underline{below the age of 21}.
    \end{example}
    \item a compound noun referring to a group of people whose age is more or less than a particular number, which consists of \textit{over} or \textit{under} followed by the plural form of the particular number.
    \begin{example}
        The \underline{over-sixties} do not want to be turned out of their homes.
        
        Schooling for \underline{under-fives} should be expanded.
    \end{example}
    [AE] This construction is not common in American English.
\end{itemize}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.261} You can put several of the above structures after a noun to talk about the age of a person or thing.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a woman \underline{in her early 30s}.
    
    $\ldots$ help for elderly ladies \underline{over 65}.
    
    She had 4 children \underline{under the age of 5}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.262} If you want to say that someone's age is similar to someone else's age, you use structures e.g. \textit{of his age} and \textit{of her parents's age} after a noun.

The \textit{of} is often dropped.
\begin{example}
    A lot of girls \underline{of Helen's age} are interested in clothes.
    
    It's easy to make friends because you're with people \underline{of your own age}.
    
    She will have a tough time when she plays with children \underline{her own age}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.263} If you want to say what the age of a thing is, you can use
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textit{be} followed by a number followed by \textit{years old}
    \begin{example}
        It'\underline{s} at least \underline{a thousand million years old}.
        
        The house \underline{was} about \underline{30 years old}.
    \end{example}
    Note that you can also use this pattern after a noun.
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ rocks \underline{200 million years old}.
    \end{example}
    \item a compound adjective indicating the century when something existed or was made, which consists of an ordinal number and \textit{century}
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a \underline{6th-century} church.
        
        $\ldots$ life in \underline{5th-century} Athens.
    \end{example}
    \item a compound adjective, usually hyphenated, consisting of a number, followed by a singular noun referring to a period of time, followed by \textit{old}
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a \underline{1,000-year-old} temple.
    \end{example}
\end{itemize}

\section{Approximate amounts \& measurements}
\noindent\fbox{2.264} If you do not know the exact number, size, or quantity of something, you can give an approximate amount of measurement using 1 of a group of special words and expressions.

Some of these words and expressions are put in front of a quantity and some are put after it.

%
Here is a list of some of the words and expressions used to give approximate amounts and measurements:
\begin{example}
    about, almost, a maximum of, a minimum of, approximately, around, at least, at most, at the maximum, at the most, less than, maximum, minimum, more than, nearly, no more than, odd, or less, or more, or so, or thereabouts, or under, over, roughly, some, something like, under, upto
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.265} Some of these expressions indicate that a number is a minimum figure and that the actual figure is or may be larger.

%
Here is a list of expressions that indicate a minimum number:
\begin{example}
    a minimum of, a least, minimum, more than, or more, over, plus
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.266} [i] You put \textit{a minimum of, more than}, and \textit{over} in front of a number.
\begin{example}
    He needed \underline{a minimum of} 26 Democratic votes.
    
    $\ldots$ a school with \underline{more than} 1300 pupils.
    
    The British have been on the island for \underline{over} a thousand years.
\end{example}
You put \textit{or more} and \textit{plus} after a number or amount, and \textit{minimum} after an amount.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a choice of 3 \underline{or more} possibilities.
    
    This is the worst disaster I can remember in my 25 years \underline{plus} as a police officer.
    
    He does an hour's homework per night \underline{minimum}.
\end{example}
You put \textit{at least} in front of a number or after a number or amount.
\begin{example}
    She had \underline{at least} a dozen brandies.
    
    I must have slept 12 hours \underline{at least}!
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.267} Some of these expressions are used to indicate that a number is a maximum figure and that the actual figure is or may be smaller.

%
Here is a list of expressions that indicate a maximum number:
\begin{example}
    almost, a maximum of, at most, at the maximum, at the most, fewer than, less than, maximum, nearly, no more than, or less, or under, under, up to
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.2608} [i] You put \textit{almost, a maximum of, fewer than, less than, nearly, no more than, under} and \textit{up to} in front of a number.

%
\textit{Fewer than} and \textit{less than} mean the same, but \textit{fewer than} is more formal.
\begin{example}
    There were \underline{fewer than} 20 people at the event.
    
    The puppy's \underline{less than} 7 weeks old.
    
    The company now supplies \underline{almost} 100 of the city's restaurants.
    
    These loans must be repaid over \underline{a maximum of} 3 years.
    
    She had \underline{nearly} 50 dollars.
    
    We managed to finish the entire job in \underline{under} 3 months.
    
    Their bodies might be \underline{up to} a meter wide.
\end{example}
You put \textit{at the maximum, at most, at the most, maximum, or less}, and \textit{or under} after a number.
\begin{example}
    Classes are of 8 \underline{at the maximum}.
    
    The images take 36 hours \underline{maximum}.
    
    The area would yield only 200 pounds of rice \underline{or less}.
    
    $\ldots$ 12 hours a week \underline{or under}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.269} Some of these expressions are used to show that a number is approximate and that the actual figure could be larger or smaller.

%
Here is a list of the expressions showing that a number is approximate:
\begin{example}
    about, approximately, around, odd, or so, or thereabouts, roughly, some, something like
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.270} [i] You put \textit{about, approximately, around, roughly, some}, and \textit{something like} in front of a number.
\begin{example}
    \underline{About} 85 students were there.
    
    Every year we have \underline{approximately} 40 pupils who take mathematics.
    
    It would cost \underline{around} 35 million pounds.
    
    A loft conversion costs \underline{roughly} \pounds12,000.
    
    They have to pay America \underline{some} \$683,000 this year.
    
    Harrington has cheated us out of \underline{something like} 30,000 quid over the past 2 years.
\end{example}
You put \textit{odd} and \textit{or so} after a number or amount, and \textit{or thereabouts} after an amount.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a hundred \underline{odd} acres.
    
    For half a minute \underline{or so}, neither of them spoke.
    
    Get the temperature to $30^\circ$ \underline{or thereabouts}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.271} You show a range of numbers using \textit{between} and \textit{and}, or \textit{from} and \textit{to}, or just \textit{to}.
\begin{example}
    Most of the farms around here are \underline{between 4 and 5 hundred} years old.
    
    My hospital groups contain \underline{from 10 to 20} patients.
    
    $\ldots$ peasants owning \underline{2 to 5} acres of land.
\end{example}
Note the use of \textit{anything} before \textit{between} and \textit{from}, to emphasize how great the range is.
\begin{example}
    An average rate of \underline{anything between 25 and 60\%} is usual.
    
    It is a job that takes \underline{anything from 2 to 5 weeks}.
\end{example}

\section{Expanding the noun phrase}
\noindent\fbox{2.272} This section deals with structures that are used to add further information about the person or thing referred to.

These are called \textbf{qualifiers}.

The word that is qualified is usually a noun but can be an indefinite pronoun or \textit{those}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.273} The structures that are dealt with in this section are
\begin{example}
    \item prepositional phrases
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a girl \underline{with red hair}.
        
        $\ldots$ the man \underline{in the dark glasses}.
    \end{example}
    The use of prepositional phrases to expand the noun phrase is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.275--2.290}.
    \item adjectives followed by phrases or clauses
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ machinery \underline{capable of clearing rubble off the main roads}.
        
        $\ldots$ the type of comments \underline{likely to provoke criticism}.
        
        $\ldots$ a concept \underline{inconceivable a hundred years earlier}.
    \end{example}
    The use of adjectives followed by phrases or clauses to expand the noun phrase is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.291--2.292}.
    \item non-finite clauses
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a simple device \underline{to test lung function}.
        
        $\ldots$ 2 of the problems \underline{mentioned above}.
        
        He gestured towards the 3 cards \underline{lying on the table}.
    \end{example}
    The use of non-finite clauses to expand the noun phrase is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.293--2.301}.
    \item noun phrases giving further information about other noun phrases.
    
    This is explained in paragraph \textbf{2.302}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.274} Some other structures are also used.

These are explained fully in other sections.

They include
\begin{itemize}
    \item single words e.g. \textit{galore} and \textit{concerned}, which are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.58--2.62}.
    \item relative clauses
    \begin{example}
        Shortly after the shooting, the man \underline{who had done it} was arrested.
        
        Where's that cake \underline{your mother made}?
    \end{example}
    \item place adverbs and time adverbials
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ down in the dungeon \underline{beneath}.
        
        $\ldots$ a reflection of life \underline{today} in England.
    \end{example}
    Time adverbials are explained in Chap. 4 and adverbs of place are explained in paragraphs \textbf{6.53--6.72}.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Nouns with prepositional phrases}
\noindent\fbox{2.275} In general, any prepositional phrase that describes classifies something can be used directly after a noun or pronoun.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the main \underline{in charge}.
    
    $\ldots$ a film about 4 men \underline{on holiday}.
    
    She reached into the room \underline{behind her}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.276} In particular, there are several kinds of prepositional phrase that are usually only used in this way.

Of these, prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{of} are the most numerous.

Others include certain uses of \textit{with, in} and \textit{by}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.277} Many nouns referring to things and actions can be expanded by using prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{of} after them.

This allows the noun to be expanded with a wide range of meanings.

You can use \textit{of} with nouns referring to feelings e.g. \textit{love} and \textit{fear} to show what the feeling relates to; e.g., \textit{fear of lying} and \textit{love of animals}.

Further meanings are described in the following paragraphs.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.278} [!] \textbf{Personal pronouns} are not usually placed after \textit{of}.

E.g., you cannot say \textit{Joyce was the daughter of him} or \textit{the pages of it}.

\textbf{Possessive determiners} are used instead to show possession.

These are explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.194--1.210}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.279} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{of} can be used to show what something consists of.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a letter \underline{of confirmation}.
    
    $\ldots$ strong feelings \underline{of jealousy}.
\end{example}
They can also be used to show what the subject matter of something is.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a picture \underline{of a house}.
    
    $\ldots$ Gretchen's account \underline{of her interview with Nichols}.
    
    $\ldots$ the idea \underline{of death}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.280} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{of} can be used to say that something belongs to or is associated with someone or something.
\begin{example}
    Cental is a trademark \underline{of Monotore Ltd}.
    
    No.28 was the town house \underline{of Sir Winston Churchill}.
    
    James is the son \underline{of a Methodist minister}.
    
    The acting ability \underline{of the pupils} is admirable.
    
    $\ldots$ the beauty \underline{of the Welsh landscape}.
    
    4 boys sat on the floor \underline{of the living room}.
    
    Ellen aimlessly turned the pages \underline{of her magazine}.
\end{example}
Note that apostrophe \textit{s} ('s) structures are much more frequently used to say that something belongs to someone or something.

\textbf{Apostrophe \textit{s}} (\textbf{'s}) is explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.211--1.222}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.281} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{of} can be used to say that someone or something has a particular quality.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a woman \underline{of energy and ambition}.
    
    $\ldots$ problems \underline{of varying complexity}.
    
    $\ldots$ a flower \underline{of monstrous proportions}.
    
    A household \underline{of this size} inevitably has problems.
\end{example}
\textit{Of} can also be used in front of a number to indicate someone's age.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a woman \underline{of 22}.
    
    $\ldots$ a child \underline{of 6}.
\end{example}
Other ways of talking about age are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.258--2.263}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.282} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{of} can be used with nouns referring to an action to show who or what is performing the action.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the arrival \underline{of the police}.
    
    $\ldots$ the growth \underline{of modern industry}.
\end{example}
They are also used to show who or what someone does something to.

E.g., if you are talking about people who support a scheme, you can call them \textit{the supporters of the scheme}.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ supporters \underline{of the hunger strike}.
    
    $\ldots$ critics \underline{of the Trade Union Movement}.
    
    $\ldots$ the creator \underline{of the universe}.
    
    $\ldots$ a student \underline{of English}.
    
    $\ldots$ the cause \underline{of the tragedy}.
\end{example}
\textit{Of} structures can also used to indicate the thing affected by an action.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the destruction \underline{of their city}.
    
    $\ldots$ the dismissal \underline{of hundreds of workers}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.283} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{of} and containing measurement are used to show how great an area, speed, distance, or temperature is.
\begin{example}
    There were fires burning over a total area \underline{of about 600 square miles}.
    
    It can barely maintain a speed \underline{of 25 kilometers an hour}.
    
    $\ldots$ an average annual temperature \underline{of $20^\circ$}.
\end{example}
Ways of measuring things are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.250--2.257}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.284} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{with} can be used to say that someone or something has a particular characteristic, feature, or possession.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a girl \underline{with red hair}.
    
    $\ldots$ a girl \underline{with a foreign accent}.
    
    $\ldots$ a big car \underline{with reclining seats}.
    
    $\ldots$ a man \underline{with a violent temper}.
    
    $\ldots$ the man \underline{with the gun}.
    
    $\ldots$ those \underline{with large families}.
\end{example}
They are also used to indicate what something has on or in it.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a sheet of paper \underline{with writing on it}.
    
    $\ldots$ a round box \underline{with some buttons in it}.
    
    $\ldots$ a white, plain envelope \underline{with her name printed on it}.
    
    $\ldots$ fragments of wrapping paper \underline{with bits of sticky tape still adhering to them}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.285} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{in} can be used to say what someone is wearing.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a grey-haired man \underline{in a raincoat}.
    
    $\ldots$ the man \underline{in the dark glasses}.
    
    $\ldots$ little groups of people \underline{in black}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.286} Prepositional phrases beginning with \textit{by} can be used after a noun referring to an action to say who or what is performing it.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ his appointment \underline{by the King}.
    
    $\ldots$ the compression of air \underline{by the piston}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.287} Some nouns, especially abstract nouns, need to be followed by a prepositional phrase to show what they relate to.

There is often little or no choice about which preposition to use after a particular noun.
\begin{example}
    He has an allergy \underline{to peanuts}.
    
    $\ldots$ his authority \underline{over them}.
    
    $\ldots$ the solution \underline{to our energy problem}.
    
    $\ldots$ the bond \underline{between mother and child}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.288} Here is a list of nouns that usually or often have \textit{to} after them:
\begin{example}
    access, addiction, adherence, affront, allegiance, allergy, allusion, alternative, answer, antidote, approach, attachment, aversion, contribution, damage, devotion, disloyalty, exception, fidelity, incitement, introduction, preface, prelude, recourse, reference, relevance, reply, resistance, return, sequel, solution, susceptibility, testimony, threat, vulnerability, witness
\end{example}
Here is a list of nouns that usually or often have \textit{for} after them:
\begin{example}
    admiration, appetite, aptitude, bid, craving, credit, cure, demand, desire, disdain, dislike, disregard, disrespect, hunger, love, need, provision, quest, recipe, regard, remedy, respect, responsibility, room, search, substitute, sympathy, synonym, taste, thirst, demand
\end{example}
Here is a list of nouns that usually or often have \textit{on} after them:
\begin{example}
    assault, attack, ban, claim, comment, concentration, constraint, crackdown, curb, dependence, effect, embargo, hold, insistence, reflection, reliance, restriction, stance, tax
\end{example}
Here is a list of nouns that usually or often have \textit{with} after them:
\begin{example}
    affinity, collision, collusion, connection, contrast, correspondence, date, dealings, dissatisfaction, encounter, familiarity, identification, intersection, intimacy, involvement, link, parity, quarrel, relationship, sympathy
\end{example}
Here is a list of nouns that are usually followed by 1 of 2 prepositions.

The list indicates the choice of prepositions available:
\begin{example}
    agreement about/on, argument against/for, battle against/for, case against/for, debate about/on, decision about/on, transition from/to
\end{example}
Here is a list of other nouns that are usually followed by a preposition.
\begin{example}
    complex about, crime against, grudge against, insurance against, reaction against, safeguard against, anger at, bond between, departure from, escape from, excerpt from, freedom from, quotation from, foray into, relapse into, awareness of, authority over, control over
\end{example}
As you can see from the lists and examples given above, it is often the case that words with a similar meaning are typically followed by the same preposition.

E.g., \textit{appetite, craving, desire, hunger}, and \textit{thirst} are all followed by \textit{for}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.289} [i] Some nouns are related to verbs that are always or often followed by a particular preposition.

These nouns are followed by the same preposition as their related verbs, and they are used to indicate the thing that is affected by the action.

E.g., \textit{to} is used after both the verb \textit{refer} and the related noun \textit{reference}.
\begin{example}
    We have already \underline{referred to} this phenomenon.
    
    $\ldots$ reverent \underline{references to} the importance of home.
    
    They swim about busily \underline{searching for} food.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{search for} food.
    
    I want to \underline{escape from} here.
    
    $\ldots$ an \underline{escape from} reality.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.290} Some nouns referring to a feeling or state are related to an adjective that is usually followed by a preposition.

These nouns are followed by the same preposition as their related adjectives.

E.g., \textit{of} is used after the adjective \textit{aware} and the related noun \textit{awareness}.
\begin{example}
    She was quite \underline{aware of} her current situation.
    
    $\ldots$ the public's increasing \underline{awareness of} the problems.
    
    He was \underline{angry at} Sally Gardner for accusing him.
    
    $\ldots$ her \underline{anger at} the kids.
\end{example}

\subsection{Nouns with adjectives}
\noindent\fbox{2.291} When adjectives are used in clauses after nouns or pronouns to expand their meaning they can be followed by
\begin{itemize}
    \item prepositional phrases
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a warning to people \underline{eager for a quick cure}.
        
        those \underline{responsible for the project}.
    \end{example}
    \item a \textit{to}-infinitive
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ remarks \underline{likely to cause offence}.
        
        It has been directed against those \underline{least able to retaliate}.
    \end{example}
    \item expression of time or place
    \begin{example}
        $\ldots$ a concept \underline{inconceivable 100 years earlier}.
        
        For the facilities \underline{available here}, I must ask for a fee.
    \end{example}
\end{itemize}
Note that you can use adjectives as qualifiers when they are preceded by time or measurement expressions.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ those \underline{still alive}.
    
    $\ldots$ a small hill \underline{about 400 feet high}.
\end{example}
Note also that a few adjectives, e.g. \textit{present} and \textit{responsible}, can be used on their own after a noun or pronoun.

The use of these adjectives is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.58--2.62}.

%
\noindent\fbox{2.292} There are some other structures, especially those that indicate comparison, degree, or result, that often involve a qualifying structure.

In particular, some words that modify adjectives, e.g. \textit{more, too}, or \textit{so}, often have a qualifying structure to complete their meaning.
\begin{example}
    Peter came in, \underline{more excited than anyone had seen him before}.
    
    Ralph was \underline{too angry to think clearly}.
    
    $\ldots$ steel cylinders strong enough to survive a nuclear catastrophe.
    
    $\ldots$ a grand piano \underline{as big as 2 coffins}.
    
    She was \underline{so ill that she couldn't eat}.
    
    Technology has made \underline{such spectacular advances that it is difficult to keep up}.
\end{example}
The use of comparative adjectives plus \textit{than} after noun phrases is explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.106--2.108}.

Other ways of comparing things are explained in paragraphs \textbf{2.123--2.139}.

The use of \textit{so$\ldots$ that} and \textit{such$\ldots$ that} is explained in paragraphs \textbf{8.58--8.63}.

\subsection{Nouns followed by \textit{to}-infinitive, \textit{-ed} participle, or \textit{-ing} participle: \textit{something to eat, a girl called Patricia, a basket containing eggs}}
\noindent\fbox{2.293} The following \textbf{non-finite} clauses ($=$ clauses containing a verb that has no tense) can be used to expand the meaning of a noun: \textit{to}-infinitive clauses (see paragraphs \textbf{2.294--2.299}), \textit{-ed} participle clauses (see paragraph \textbf{2.300}), and \textit{-ing} participle clauses (see paragraph \textbf{2.301}).

%
\noindent\fbox{2.294} A \textit{to}-infinitive clause is often placed after nouns in order to show what the thing referred to is intended to do.
\begin{example}
    The government of Mexico set up a programme \underline{to develop new varieties of wheat}.
    
    They need people \underline{to work in the factories}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.295} You can refer to something or someone that should or can have something done to them by using a clause containing a \textit{to}-infinitive after a noun or indefinite pronoun.
\begin{example}
    I make notes in the back of my diary of things \underline{to be mended or replaced}.
    
    $\ldots$ When I've had something \underline{to eat}.
\end{example}
You can also use a clause consisting of a \textit{to}-infinitive followed by a preposition.
\begin{example}
    There wasn't even a chair \underline{to sit on}.
    
    He had nothing \underline{to write with}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.296} You can also use a \textit{to}-infinitive clause when you want to say that you are talking about, e.g., the 1st, oldest, or only person who did something.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the 1st woman \underline{to be elected to the council}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.297} A clause containing a \textit{to}-infinitive is used after some abstract nouns to show what action they relate to.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ people who didn't have the opportunity \underline{to go to university}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.298} [i] Many of these nouns are related to verbs or adjectives that are also often followed by \textit{to}-infinitive clauses.

E.g., a \textit{to}-infinitive clause is used after both the verb \textit{need} and the noun \textit{need}, and after both the adjective \textit{able} and the related noun \textit{ability}.
\begin{example}
    I \underline{need to borrow} 5000 dollars.
    
    $\ldots$ the \underline{need to preserve} secrecy about their intentions.
    
    It \underline{failed to grow}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.299} Here is a list of nouns that usually or often have a \textit{to}-infinitive clause after them:
\begin{example}
    ability, attempt, bid, chance, compulsion, desire, disinclination, failure, inability, inclination, need, opportunity, readiness, reason, refusal, unwillingness, urge, way, willingness
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.300} A clause containing an \textit{-ed} participle can be used directly after a noun to show that something has been produced or affected by an action.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ a girl \underline{called Patricia}.
    
    $\ldots$ dresses \underline{made of paper}.
    
    $\ldots$ 2 of the problems \underline{mentioned above}.
    
    $\ldots$ a story \underline{written by a 9-year-old girl}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.301} A clause containing an \textit{-ing} participle can be used directly after a noun to show that someone or something is doing something.
\begin{example}
    He gestured towards the 3 cards \underline{lying on the table}.
    
    $\ldots$ a wicker shopping-basket \underline{containing groceries}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{2.302} You can give further information about someone or something by using a noun phrase that describes them or identifies them.

%
If you put this noun phrase after the main noun phrase, a comma is almost always put after the main noun phrase because the 2nd noun phrase is separate from it, not part of it.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ the bald eagle, \underline{the symbol of America}.
    
    $\ldots$ David Beckham, \underline{a 1st-class football player}.
    
    Her mother, \underline{a Canadian}, died when she was 6.
\end{example}
If you put this noun phrase before the main noun phrase, you can sometimes choose whether to use a comma to separate the 2 noun phrases or not.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ \underline{Joan's husband}, Jim Inglis.
    
    $\ldots$ \underline{my husband} George.
\end{example}

%------------------------------------------------------------------------------%

\chapter{Making a message: types of verb}

\noindent\fbox{3.1} When you make a statement, you use a \textbf{clause}.

A clause that is used to make a statement contains a \textbf{noun phrase}, which refers to the person or thing that you are talking about, and a \textbf{verb phrase}, which shows what short of action, process, or state you are talking about.

%
The noun phrase, which usually comes in front of the verb, is called the \textbf{subject} or the verb or the clause.

E.g., in the clause \textit{Ellen laughed, Ellen} is the subject.

The information of noun phrases is explained in \textbf{Chaps. 1--2}.

%
A verb phrase used in a statement has a particular form, and shows agreement with number and person.

The formation of verb phrases is explained in the Reference section.

Quite often in statements the verb phrase is a single word, and it is quite common to talk about the \textit{verb} of a clause.

%
This chapter deals mainly with the use of the verbs in \textbf{active} clauses, where the subject is the performer of an action rather than the person or thing affected by an action.

The use of verbs in \textbf{passive} clauses, where the subject is the person or thing affected, is explained in paragraphs \textbf{9.8--9.24}.

\section{Show who is involved}
\noindent\fbox{3.2} If an action or event involves only 1 person or thing, you mention only the performer of the action (the subject) and the action (the verb).
\begin{example}
    The girl screamed.
    
    I waited.
    
    An awful thing has happened.
\end{example}
Verbs like this are called \textbf{intransitive verbs}.

%
However, you can then mention another person or thing that is involved using a \textbf{prepositional phrase}.
\begin{example}
    She walked \underline{across the street}.
\end{example}
Intransitive verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.8--3.13}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.3} If the action or event involves another person or thing that the action affects, relates to, or produces, you put a noun phrase referring to them after the verb.

This is called the \textbf{object} of the verb.

If it is necessary to distinguish it from other objects, it is called the \textbf{direct object}.
\begin{example}
    He closed \underline{the door}.
    
    I hate \underline{sport}.
    
    Some of the women noticed \underline{me}.
\end{example}
Verbs like this are called \textbf{transitive verbs}.

Transitive verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.14--3.25}.

%
\textbf{Reflexive verbs} and \textbf{delexical verbs} are special kinds of transitive verbs.

They are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.26--3.31} and \textbf{3.32--3.45}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.4} The majority of verbs in English give you the option of presenting an event either as involving the subject only, or as involving the subject and someone or something else as a direct object.
\begin{example}
    She \underline{paints} by holding the brush in her teeth.
    
    Yarkov \underline{paints vivid portraits of friends and acquaintances}.
    
    Gus asked me whether I'd like to have dinner with him. I \underline{accepted}.
    
    I \underline{accepted the invitation}.
\end{example}
This means that most verbs can be used with or without an object.

Verbs that can be used like this are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.46--3.54}.

%
With some verbs, the thing affected by the action can be put as the object of the verb or as the object of a preposition after the verb.

These verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.55--3.58}.

%
\textbf{Ergative verbs} are a special kind of verb that can be used with or without an object.

They are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.59--3.67}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.5} \textbf{Reciprocal verbs} refer to actions that involve people affecting each other in the same way with the same action.

There are 2 types of \textbf{reciprocal verb}.

1 type is used either with or without an object.
\begin{example}
    We \underline{met} at Hargreaves' place.
    
    I \underline{had met him} in Zermatt.
\end{example}
The other type is used without an object, and with or without a prepositional phrase mentioning 1 of the participants.
\begin{example}
    We \underline{argued} over this question for a long time.
    
    I \underline{argued with this man} for half an hour.
\end{example}
Reciprocal verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.68--3.72}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.6} Some transitive verbs also allow you to mention a person who benefits from an action or receives something as a result.

The verb is then followed by both a direct object and an \textbf{indirect object}.
\begin{example}
    Hand \underline{me my bad}.
    
    His uncle had given \underline{him books on India}.
    
    She sends \underline{you her love}.
    
    She passed \underline{him his cup}.
\end{example}
Verbs that can take an indirect object as well as a direct object are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.73--3.82}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.7} Some verbs have 2 or 3 parts.

These are \textbf{phrasal verbs} and \textbf{compound verbs}.

Phrasal verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.83--3.116}, and compound verbs are explained in paragraphs \textbf{3.117--3.125}.

\subsection{Intransitive verbs: talking about events that involve only the subject}
\noindent\fbox{3.8} When you are talking about an action or event that does not have an object, you use an \textbf{intransitive verb}.
\begin{example}
    Her whole body \underline{ached}.
    
    Such people still \underline{exist}.
    
    My condition \underline{deteriorated}.
\end{example}
Many intransitive verbs describe physical behavior or making of sounds.
\begin{example}
    Bob \underline{coughed}.
    
    Vicki \underline{wept} bitterly.
    
    The gate \underline{squeaked}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.9} Here is a list of verbs that are normally used without an object and that usually or often have no adverb or prepositional phrase after them:
\begin{example}
    ache, advance, arise, arrive, bleed, blush, cease, collapse, cough, crackle, cry, decay, depart, deteriorate, die, digress, dine, disappear, disintegrate, doze, droop, economize, elapse, ensue, erupt, evaporate, exist, expire, faint, fall, falter, fidget, flinch, flourish, fluctuate, gleam, growl, happen, hesitate, howl, itch, kneel, laugh, moan, occur, pause, persist, prosper, quiver, recede, relent, rise, roar, scream, shine, shiver, sigh, sleep, slip, smile, snarl, sneeze, snore, snort, sob, sparkle, speak, squeak, squeal, stink, subside, sulk, surrender, swim, throb, tingle, vanish, vary, vibrate, wait, waver, weep, wilt, work, yawn
\end{example}
A few of these verbs are used with an object in idioms or with very specific objects, but they are intransitive in all their common meanings.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.10} Many intransitive verbs always or typically have an adverb or prepositional phrase after them.

With some, only a prepositional phrase beginning with a particular preposition is possible.

This use of a preposition allows something affected by the action to be mentioned, as the object of the preposition.
\begin{example}
    Everything you see here \underline{belongs to} me.
    
    Landlords often \underline{resorted to} violence.
    
    I \underline{sympathized with} them.
    
    I\underline{'m relying on} Bill.
    
    He \underline{strives for} excellence in all things.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.11} Here is a list of verbs that always or typically have a particular preposition after them when they are used with a particular meaning:
\begin{example}
    rave about
    
    insure against, plot against, react against
    
    hint at
    
    alternate between, differentiate between, oscillate between
    
    appeal for, atone for, care for, clamor for, hope for, long for, opt for, pay for, qualify for, strive for, yearn for
    
    detract from, emanate from, emerge from, radiate from, shrink from, stem from, suffer from
    
    believe in, consist in, culminate in, dabble in, indulge in, invest in, result in, wallow in
    
    lapse into
    
    complain of, conceive of, consist of, despair of, learn of, smack of, think of, tire of
    
    bet on, feed on, insist on, spy on, trample on
    
    adhere to, allude to, amount to, appeal to, aspire to, assent to, attend to, belong to, bow to, cling to, defer to, dictate to, lead to, listen to, object to, refer to, relate to, resort to, revert to, stoop to
    
    alternate with, associate with, consort with, contend with, flirt with, grapple with, sympathize with, teem with
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs that can have either of 2 prepositions after them with the same or very similar meaning:
\begin{example}
    abound in/with, cater for/to, conform to/with, contribute to/towards, depend on/upon, dote on/upon, embark on/upon, end in/with, engage in/on, enthuse about/over, gravitate to/towards, hunger after/for, improve on/upon, liaise between/with, lust after/for, prevail on/upon, profit by/from, rely on/upon, revolve around/round, spring from/out of
\end{example}
Note that some intransitive verbs can be used in the passive when they are followed by a preposition.

See paragraph \textbf{9.23}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.12} Other verbs can be followed by a variety of prepositional phrases, or an adverb, often relating to time or place.

%
Verbs of movements are usually or often followed by adverbs or phrases relating to direction.
\begin{example}
    He went \underline{back to his own room}.
    
    I traveled \underline{south}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs of movement:
\begin{example}
    come, crawl, creep, drift, flow, gallop, glide, go, hurtle, plunge, run, soar, spring, stroll, travel, walk
\end{example}
\textit{Look, gaze, glance}, and \textit{stare} are also followed by adverbs or phrases relating to direction.

%
Verbs of position are usually followed by adverbs or phrases relating to position.
\begin{example}
    Donald was lying \underline{on the bed}.
    
    She lives \underline{in Lausanne}.
    
    I used to live \underline{here}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs of position:
\begin{example}
    be, belong, hand, lie, live, be located, remain, sit, be situated, stand, stay
\end{example}
Verbs e.g. \textit{extend} or \textit{stretch} are followed by adverbs or phrases relating to extent.
\begin{example}
    $\ldots$ an area stretching \underline{from London to Cambridge}.
\end{example}
There are a few verbs that are always followed by other types of adverb or phrase.
\begin{example}
    It behaves \underline{rather like a squirrel}.
    
    My brother agreed to act \underline{as a go-between}.
    
    I hoped that the absorption of poison hadn't progressed \underline{too far}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs that are always followed by other types of adverb or phrase:
\begin{example}
    act, behave, campaign, progress
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.13} There are a few verbs that are usually intransitive but that can be transitive when they are used with 1 particular object.

The object is usually directly related to the verb.

E.g., \textit{smile} is usually used without an object, but you can use it with the noun \textit{smile}.

E.g., \textit{He smiled a patient smile} is a literary alternative to saying \textit{He smiled patiently}.

The focus is on the type of smile rather than on the act of smiling.
\begin{example}
    Steve \underline{smiled his thin, cruel smile}.
    
    He appears to have \underline{lived the life of any other rich gentleman}.
    
    Alice \underline{laughed a scornful laugh}.
    
    I once \underline{dreamed a very nice dream} about you.
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs that can only be used with an object when the object is directly related to the verb:
\begin{example}
    dance (a dance), die (a death), dream (a dream), laugh (a laugh), live (a life), sigh (a sigh), smile (a smile)
\end{example}
A more common way of focusing on the noun phrase is to use a \textbf{delexical verb} e.g. \textit{give, take}, or \textit{have}, as in \textit{Mary gave him a really lovely smile}.

See paragraphs \textbf{3.32--3.45} for more information about the use of delexical verbs.

\subsection{Transitive verbs: involving someone or something other than the subject}
\noindent\fbox{3.14} Many verbs describe events that must, in addition to the subject, involve someone or something else.

Some of these verbs can only be used with a following object.
\begin{example}
    The extra profit \underline{justifies} the investment.
    
    He \underline{had committed} a disgraceful action.
    
    They are \underline{employing} more staff.
\end{example}
This means that they are followed by a direct object.
\begin{example}
    She had \underline{friends}.
    
    Children seek \underline{independence}.
    
    The trial raised \underline{a number of questions}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.15} Many verbs that are only used with an object can take a large range of objects.

E.g., there are many things you can \textit{want}: money, a rest, success, and so on.
\begin{example}
    She \underline{wanted} some help.
    
    I \underline{put} my hand on the door.
    
    She \underline{described} her background.
    
    I still \underline{support} the government.
    
    He \underline{had} always \underline{liked} Mr Phillips.
    
    Japan \underline{has} a population of about a hundred million.
\end{example}
Some transitive verbs have a restricted range of objects, because of their meaning.

E.g., the object of the verb \textit{kill} must be something that is alive.

The object of the verb \textit{waste} must be something you ca use, e.g. time, money, or food.
\begin{example}
    They \underline{killed} huge elephants with tiny poisoned darts.
    
    Why \underline{waste} money on them?
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.16} Here is a list of verbs that are transitive:
\begin{example}
    achieve, address, admire, affect, afford, avoid, bear, believe, blame, build, buy, calm, carry, catch, claim, commit, complete, concern, consider, control, convince, correct, cover, create, cut, damage, defy, demand, describe, design, desire, destroy, discover, discuss, display, do, dread, enjoy, equal, exchange, expect, experience, express, favor, fear, fill, find, free, get, give, grant, guard, handle, hate, have, hear, heat, hire, hit, include, influence, introduce, issue, justify, keep, kill, know, lack, like, list, love, lower, maintain, make, mean, mention, name, need, own, plant, please, prefer, prevent, process, produce, pronounce, protect, provide, raise, reach, receive, recommend, record, release, remember, remove, rent, report, respect, reveal, risk, see, seek, sell, shock, specify, spot, support, take, tease, test, threaten, trust, upset, use, value, want, waste, wear, welcome
\end{example}
Note that \textit{do} and \textit{have} are also very often used as \textbf{auxiliaries}.

See the Reference section for this us.

%
\textit{Have got} and \textit{has got} are often used instead of the present tense of \textit{have} when talking about possession.

The forms of \textit{have} like auxiliaries when used like this before \textit{got}.
\begin{example}
    I\underline{'ve got} an umbrella.
    
    She\underline{'s got} a degree.
\end{example}
\textit{Measure} and \textit{weigh} are sometimes considered to be transitive verbs when used to state measurements and weights.

This use is explained in paragraph \textbf{2.252}.

\textit{Cost} is used to state the cost of something, as in \textit{An adult ticket costs 90p}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.17} When you are talking about something that affects a person rather than a thing, it is normal in English to say who that person is.

Therefore, verbs e.g. \textit{anger, thank}, and \textit{warn}, which involve affecting people, usually have a human object.
\begin{example}
    I tried to \underline{comfort} her.
    
    Her sudden death \underline{had surprised} everybody.
    
    Blue \underline{suits} you.
    
    Money did not \underline{interest} him very much.
    
    Lebel \underline{briefed} Caron on the events of th afternoon.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.18} Here is a list of verbs that usually have a human object:
\begin{example}
    anger, brief, comfort, contact, frighten, interest, suit, surprise, tease, thank, trouble, warn
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.19} With some transitive verbs, you have to give additional information about what is going on by using an adverb or prepositional phrase after the object.

%
Some verbs typically have a prepositional phrase beginning with a particular preposition after their object.
\begin{example}
    The judge \underline{based} his decision \underline{on constitutional rights}.
    
    He \underline{had subjected} me \underline{to the pressure of financial ruin}.
    
    My parents still \underline{view} me \underline{as a little boy}.
\end{example}
%
Here is a list of verbs that always or usually have a particular preposition after their object:
\begin{example}
    regard as, view as
    
    mistake for, swap for
    
    dissociate from, prevent from
    
    deprive of, remind of, rid of, rob of
    
    accustom to, ascribe to, attribute to, compare to, condemn to, confine to, consign to, dedicate to, entitle to, liken to, owe to, return to, subject to, subordinate to
    
    acquaint with, associate with, confront with, engrave with, pelt with, ply with, trust with
\end{example}
With the following verbs, there is a choice of preposition:
\begin{example}
    divide by/into
    
    incorporate in/into
    
    based on/upon, lavish on/upon
    
    entrust to/with, equate to/with, present to/with, supply to/with
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.20} Other verbs are typically followed by an adverb or prepositional phrase, but not one containing a particular preposition.

The adverb or phrase often relates to place.
\begin{example}
    He \underline{placed} the baby \underline{on the woman's lap}.
    
    I \underline{positioned} my chair \underline{outside the room}.
    
    He never \underline{puts} anything \underline{away}.
    
    He \underline{treated} his laborers \underline{with kindness}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs that usually have some kind of adverb or prepositional phrase after their object:
\begin{example}
    bring, chuck, convey, cram, direct, drag, escort, fling, hoist, job, jot, jay, lead, place, point, position, prop, put, rip, send, set, shove, smear, stick, store, throw, thrust, tie, treat
\end{example}
For more on adverbs and phrases that follow verbs, see Chap. 6.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.21} Note that some verbs of movement and position are transitive, not intransitive; they are followed by nouns referring to places rather than by adverbs or prepositional phrases.

This is because the verbs themselves show that you are talking about movement or position of a particular kind.

E.g., \textit{enter} implies movement \textit{into} a place and \textit{occupy} implies position \textit{in} a place.
\begin{example}
    He \underline{approached} the house nervously.
    
    It was dark by the time they \underline{reached} their house.
    
    A small ornamental pool \underline{occupied} the center of the room.
    
    Everyone had \underline{left} the room.
\end{example}
Here is a list of transitive verbs of movement:
\begin{example}
    approach, enter, leave, near, reach, round
\end{example}
Here is a list of transitive verbs of position:
\begin{example}
    cover, crowd, fill, inhabit, occupy, throng
\end{example}
Some verbs of movement can be followed either by a noun phrase or by a prepositional phrase.

See paragraph \textbf{3.58}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.22} [i] Note that even verbs that are almost always followed by an object can occasionally be used without an object.

This is possible in very restricted contexts.

E.g., if you are contrasting 2 actions, it is not necessary to say what else is involved.
\begin{example}
    Money markets are the places where people with money \underline{buy} and \underline{sell}.
    
    Some people \underline{build} while others \underline{destroy}.
    
    We \underline{gave}, they \underline{took}.
\end{example}
If you use a list of different verbs for emphasis, you do not need to name the object.
\begin{example}
    They set out to be rude; to \underline{defy}, \underline{threaten}, or \underline{tease}.
\end{example}
If you repeat a verb in order to contrast it with a similar action, or to emphasize it, the object can be omitted.
\begin{example}
    She had ceased \underline{to love} as she had once \underline{loved}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.23} Verbs that describe feelings and attitudes are sometimes used without an object, particularly in the \textbf{\textit{to}-infinitive} form.

This is because the object is assumed to be people in general.

E.g., \textit{please} usually requires an object, but you can say \textit{He likes to please}, meaning he likes to please people.
\begin{example}
    He likes \underline{to shock}.
    
    She was anxious \underline{to please}.
    
    He must be convinced if he is \underline{to convince}.
    
    I have a tendency \underline{to tease}.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.24} There is a large group of verbs, e.g. \textit{say, suggest}, and \textit{think}, which are used to report what people say or think.

They are called \textbf{reporting verbs}.

They are followed by a \textit{that}-clause which is called the \textbf{reported clause}.
\begin{example}
    She \underline{said} that she would come.
\end{example}
The reported clause is often thought of as being an object, and so these verbs are usually said to be transitive verbs.

In this book, reporting verbs are explained in Chap. 7.

%
Reporting verbs e.g. \textit{advise} and \textit{persuade}, which have an object that refers to the person being addressed, are explained in paragraphs \textbf{7.75--7.76}.

%
Some reporting verbs can take as their object a noun e.g. \textit{question} or \textit{story} that refers to something that is said or written.

These verbs are listed in paragraph \textbf{7.82}.

Some take an object that refers to an event or fact, and is therefore closely related to a \textit{that}-clause.

These are listed in paragraph \textbf{7.83}.

%
Verbs e.g. \textit{believe} and \textit{know} that can be used as reporting verbs, but that are ordinary transitive verbs when used with another common meaning, are included in the lists of transitive verbs given above.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.25} Most transitive verbs can be used in the passive.

See paragraphs \textbf{9.9--9.21}.

\subsection{Reflexive verbs: verbs where the object refers back to the subject}
\noindent\fbox{3.26} If you want to talk about a situation where the same person is involved as both the subject and the object of an action, you use a \textbf{reflexive pronoun} as the object of a clause.

E.g., it is common to blame someone else if something goes wrong, but you say \textit{I blame myself for what happened} if you think that the mistake was your own fault.

%
Although a few verbs are typically used with reflexive pronouns, you can actually use a reflexive pronoun as the object of any transitive verb, when the meaning allows you to do so.
\begin{example}
    I \underline{blame myself} for not paying attention.
    
    She \underline{freed herself} from my embrace.
    
    After the meeting, he \underline{introduced himself} to me.
    
    Why not buy a book and \underline{teach yourself}?
    
    \underline{Don't deceive yourself}.
    
    We \underline{must ask ourselves} several questions.
    
    Every country has the right to \underline{defend itself}.
\end{example}
\textbf{Reflexive pronouns} are explained in paragraphs \textbf{1.111--1.118}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.27} Note that the verbs \textit{busy, content}, and \textit{pride} are true reflexive verbs: they must be used with a reflexive pronoun.
\begin{example}
    He \underline{had busied himself} in the laboratory.
    
    Many scholars \underline{contented themselves} with writing textbooks.
    
    He \underline{prides himself} on his tidiness.
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.28} Another small group of verbs only take an object that refers to a person when the object is a reflexive pronoun.

E.g., you can \textit{express an opinion} and you can \textit{express yourself} (meaning that you can put ideas into words), but you cannot \textit{express a person}.
\begin{example}
    Prof. Baxendale \underline{expressed himself} very forcibly.
    
    She \underline{enjoyed herself} enormously.
    
    He \underline{applied himself} to learning how Parliament worked.
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs that take a reflexive pronoun as their object when you refer to a person:
\begin{example}
    apply, compose, distance, enjoy, excel, exert, express, strain
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.29} Some verbs that normally do not have objects, because they involve only the performer of the action, can have a reflexive pronoun as their object if you want to emphasize that the subject is doing something that affects himself or herself.

You can therefore say \textit{Bill washed himself} rather than \textit{Bill washed}.
\begin{example}
    I always \underline{wash} 5 times a day.
    
    Children were encouraged to \underline{wash themselves}.
    
    I stood in the kitchen while he \underline{shaved}.
    
    He prefers to \underline{shave himself} before breakfast.
    
    Ashton \underline{had behaved} abominably.
    
    He is old enough to \underline{behave himself}.
    
    Successful companies know how to \underline{adapt} to change.
    
    You've got to be willing to \underline{adapt yourself}.
\end{example}
Here is a list of verbs that have sense in which you can use a reflexive pronoun for emphasis:
\begin{example}
    acclimatize, adapt, behave, commit, dress, hide, move, readjust, shave, undress, wash
\end{example}
%
\noindent\fbox{3.30} [i] Note that reflexive pronouns are not used as much in English as in some other languages when talking about actions that you do to yourself.

As mentioned above, you would usually say \textit{I washed} rather than \textit{I washed myself} in English.

Sometimes a noun with a possessive is used instead.

E.g., you would say \textit{I combed my hair} rather than \textit{I combed myself}.

%
\noindent\fbox{3.31} Note that reflexive verbs are not used in the passive.
